The Two Interpretations of Power for Obedience
Presentation of two interpretations of power for obedience and the subsequent objection and defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main interpretations of 'power for obedience' (qudrat al-ṭāʿa) and the objection to al-Ashʿarī's view.

Core Idea
Power for obedience is either soundness of causes or the accident accompanying the act.

This passage delves into the disagreement about what 'power for obedience' means. Imām al-Ḥaramayn interprets it as soundness of causes and instruments, which requires additional phrases to exclude the disbeliever. Al-Ashʿarī interprets it as the accident accompanying obedience, which automatically excludes the disbeliever. An objection arises about obligation before the act, and the response involves the concept of proximate potentiality and the persistence of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the two interpretations
First, understand Imām al-Ḥaramayn's view: power is soundness of causes and instruments. Then al-Ashʿarī's view: power is the accident that accompanies the act of obedience.
Note how each view affects whether the definition of tawfiq needs extra phrases.
2
Analyze the objection to al-Ashʿarī
The objection says: if power only exists at the moment of the act, how can the person be obligated before the act? That would be imposing obligation on the incapable.
3
Examine the response
The response uses 'proximate potentiality' (al-quwwa al-qarība) – the person is capable because of soundness of causes and instruments, even if the accident of power comes later.
This response depends on al-Ashʿarī's view that accidents do not persist.
4
Note the truth about accident persistence
The commentator states that the correct view is that accidents can persist for two moments, so power can precede the act without problem.
This is a key Ashʿarī doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مُوَفِّقٌ) معطوفٌ على (خالق) بحرف عطف مقدَّرٍ ، كما أشار إليه الشارح، حيث قال: (وموفق) فقدر حرف العطف، و(موفق) مأخوذ من التوفيق، وهو لغة: التأليف بين الأشياء، وشرعاً خلق قدرة الطاعة في العبد. وهل يحتاج لقولهم : وتسهيل سبيل الخير إليه أو قولهم : والداعية إليها ؛ أي: الميل النفساني إلى الطاعة، أو لا يحتاج لذلك؟ خلافٌ مبني على الخلاف في تفسير قدرة الطاعة : ففسرها إمام الحرمين بسلامة الأسباب والآلات والمراد من الأسباب الأشياء التي تكون حاملة على الفعل، والمراد من الآلات الأشياء التي يحصل بها الإعانة على الفعل، فالماء الذي يتوضأ به من الأسباب العرفية للصلاة، والأعضاء التي تحاول بها الطاعة آلات لها، وعلى هذا التفسير فيحتاج لما ذكر لإخراج الكافر، فإنه ليس موفقاً، مع أن الله خلق فيه قدرة الطاعة بالمعنى السابق. وفسرها الأشعري بالعَرَض المقارن للطاعة، وعلى هذا التفسير فلا يحتاج لما ذكر ؛ لأن الكافر خارجٌ مِنْ أول الأمر؛ إذ لم يخلق الله فيه قدرة الطاعة بهذا المعنى. وأُورد عليه أن الشخص مكلَّف قبل الطاعة، مع أنَّه قبلها على كلامه ليس فيه قدرة، فيلزم عليه تكليف العاجز، وهو ممنوع. وأُجيب بأنه قادرٌ بالقوة القريبة لما اتصف به من سلامة الأسباب والآلات، وهذا بناء على ما قاله الأشعري من أن العَرَض كالبياض لا يبقى زمانين، بل العَرَض في هذا الزمان غير العَرَض في الزمان الذي قبله وهكذا فيكون كالماء الجاري، والحقُّ أن العَرَض يبقى زمانين وعليه فلا مانع من تقدم القدرة على الطاعة عنها.
His statement: (Muwaffiq) is coordinated to (Khāliq) by an implied conjunction, as the commentator indicated when he said: (Wa muwaffiq). He thus implied the conjunction. (Muwaffiq) is derived from tawfiq, which linguistically means harmonizing between things, and in Sacred Law means creating the power of obedience in the servant. Does it need their statement: 'and facilitating the path of good to him' or their statement: 'and the prompting (al-dā'iyah) to it' – i.e., the psychic inclination to obedience – or does it not need that? A disagreement built on the disagreement in interpreting the power of obedience. For Imām al-Ḥaramayn interpreted it as soundness of causes (asbāb) and instruments (ālāt) – the meaning of 'causes' being the things that are supportive of the act, and the meaning of 'instruments' being the things by which assistance in the act is obtained. Thus, water with which one performs ablution is among the customary causes of prayer, and the limbs with which one attempts obedience are its instruments. On this interpretation, it [the definition] needs what was mentioned to exclude the disbeliever, for he is not given success (muwaffaq), even though God created in him the power of obedience in the previous sense. And al-Ashʿarī interpreted it as the accident (al-ʿaraḍ) accompanying the act of obedience. On this interpretation, it does not need what was mentioned, because the disbeliever is excluded from the outset, since God did not create in him the power of obedience in this sense. An objection was raised against him: that a person is obligated (mukallaf) before obedience, yet before it, according to his statement, there is no power in him, so it entails imposing an obligation on the incapable, which is prohibited. And it was answered that he is capable by proximate potentiality (al-quwwa al-qarība) because he is characterized by soundness of causes and instruments. This is built on what al-Ashʿarī said: that an accident like whiteness does not persist for two moments; rather, the accident in this moment is not the accident in the preceding moment, and so on, so it is like flowing water. But the truth is that an accident persists for two moments, and accordingly there is no impediment to the power preceding the obedience.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the term 'tawfiq' (divine enabling). The commentator begins by explaining the syntax: 'muwaffiq' is coordinated to 'khāliq' with an implied conjunction. Then he defines tawfiq linguistically as harmonizing between things, and in Sacred Law as creating the power of obedience in the servant. He then raises a question: does the definition need additional phrases like 'facilitating the path of good' or 'the prompting to obedience' (i.e., the psychic inclination)? This disagreement is based on a deeper disagreement about what 'power of obedience' (qudrat al-ṭāʿa) means. The commentator then presents two interpretations: First, Imām al-Ḥaramayn interprets it as soundness of causes and instruments. He gives examples: water for ablution is a cause, limbs are instruments. On this view, the additional phrases are needed to exclude the disbeliever, because even though God creates soundness of causes and instruments in the disbeliever, he is not given success (tawfiq) because he lacks the prompting. Second, al-Ashʿarī interprets power as the accident (ʿaraḍ) that accompanies the act of obedience. On this view, the additional phrases are unnecessary because the disbeliever is automatically excluded: God does not create that accident in him. An objection is raised against al-Ashʿarī: if power is an accident that only exists with the act, then before the act the person has no power, yet he is obligated (mukallaf) – this would be imposing an obligation on the incapable, which is invalid. The answer is that the person is capable by 'proximate potentiality' (al-quwwa al-qarība), i.e., he has soundness of causes and instruments, which makes him able to perform the act. This answer depends on al-Ashʿarī's view that accidents do not persist; they are renewed every moment, so power as an accident is created at the moment of the act. The commentator then states the truth: that accidents do persist for two moments, so there is no problem with power preceding the act. The passage thus clarifies the doctrinal options regarding tawfiq.

Key Terms
قدرة الطاعة
إمام الحرمين
الأشعري
الأسباب والآلات
العرض
القوة القريبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that al-Ashʿarī's view implies that power cannot precede the act, making obligation impossible.
Correction: The passage shows that the response uses proximate potentiality, and the truth of accident persistence resolves the issue.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on which interpretation does the definition of tawfiq need the additional phrases 'facilitating the path of good' and 'the prompting to obedience'?
A) Imām al-Ḥaramayn's interpretation
B) Al-Ashʿarī's interpretation
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The two interpretations of power for obedience lead to different implications for the definition of tawfiq, and the objection to al-Ashʿarī is resolved by the concept of proximate potentiality and the persistence of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle to obey Allah, do you see yourself as lacking the 'causes and instruments' (like knowledge, health, or opportunity) or as lacking the moment-by-moment 'accident' of power that only Allah creates? Which perspective makes you turn to Him more urgently?
?
The objection says that if power only exists with the act, then obligation before the act is impossible—yet you are obligated right now, in this moment, even before your next act of worship. Does knowing that you have 'proximate potentiality' (soundness of causes and instruments) give you confidence or fear? How does it change your response to your own procrastination?
?
Al-Ashʿarī's view depends on accidents being renewed every moment, like flowing water. Your obedience today is not the same as your obedience yesterday—each moment is fresh from Allah. If you truly believed that, how would it affect your gratitude for the act you just performed and your hope for the next?

Two Interpretations of Tawfiq and the Sinning Believer
Summarizes two interpretations of tawfiq and defends the sinning believer's success.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of tawfiq and why the believing sinner is still considered given success (muwaffaq).

Core Idea
Tawfiq is act-specific; a believer may sin in one area but be enabled in another.

This lesson summarizes the key interpretations of divine enabling (tawfiq) and addresses a critical question: can a believer who sins still be considered enabled by Allah? The commentator clarifies that success is act-specific, supported by a Quranic verse and poetic wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once without analyzing; then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: summary of two opinions, then an implication, then clarification with quotes.
2
Identify the two statements (qawlān) on tawfiq
Find where the commentator presents the two interpretations. Note the difference: one includes creating the power and facilitating good or prompting; the other is only creating the power.
These are built on two interpretations of the power of obedience mentioned earlier.
3
Understand the implication and evidence
The commentator argues that restricting the exclusion to the disbeliever implies the sinning believer is muwaffaq. He then clarifies that success is act-specific and supports this with a Quranic verse and a poetic line.
Consider the quotes from al-Junayd and Ibn al-Farid as evidence that saints can sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتحصل من ذلك أن في التوفيق قولين : القول الأول : أنه خلق قدرة الطاعة في العبد، وتسهيل سبيل الخير إليه أو الداعية إليها، وفي بعض العبارات : خلق الطاعة نفسها، وهو ظاهر . والقولُ الثاني : أنه خلقُ قدرة الطاعة في العبد، وهذان القولان مبنيان على القولين في تفسير قدرة الطاعة . واقتصارهم على إخراج الكافر يقتضي أن المؤمن العاصي موفق، وهو الحق، خلافاً لمن قال : الموفق لا يعصي ؛ إذ لا قدرة له على المعصية، كما أن المخذول لا يطيع؛ إذ لا قدرة له على الطاعة، ولك أن تقول : الموفق لا يعصي من حيث ما وفق فيه، والمخذول لا يطيع من حيث ما خذل فيه . وقد سُئل الجنيد أيعصي الوليُّ؟ فأطرق، ثم رفع رأسه، وقال: ﴿وَكَانَ أَمْرُ اللَّهِ قَدَرًا مَقْدُورًا﴾ [الأحزاب: ۳۸) . ومن كلام ابن الفارض : [من مجزوء الرجز] مَنْ ذَا الَّذِي مَا سَاءَ قَط؟ وَمَنْ لَهُ الحُسْنَى فَصَارَ؟
Thus it follows that concerning tawfiq there are two statements (qawlān): The first statement: that it is creating the power of obedience in the servant, and facilitating the path of good to him or the prompting (al-dāʿiya) to it. In some expressions: creating the obedience itself, which is apparent. The second statement: that it is creating the power of obedience in the servant. These two statements are built on the two statements regarding the interpretation of the power of obedience. And their restricting [the exclusion] to the disbeliever implies that the believing sinner is given success (muwaffaq), and this is the truth, contrary to those who said: the one given success does not sin, for he has no power over disobedience, just as the one abandoned (makhdhūl) does not obey, for he has no power over obedience. And you may say: the one given success does not sin with respect to that in which he was given success, and the one abandoned does not obey with respect to that in which he was abandoned. And al-Junayd was asked: 'Does the saint (walī) sin?' He lowered his head, then raised it and said: {And the command of God is a decree determined} [al-Aḥzāb: 38]. And from the words of Ibn al-Fāriḍ: [in rajaz meter] 'Who is it that has never done evil? And who only has the good?'
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a summary and conclusion of the discussion on tawfiq. The commentator first reiterates that there are two statements (qawlān) regarding tawfiq: one that includes creating the power of obedience and either facilitating goodness or creating the prompting (dāʿiya), and another that simply creates the power of obedience. Some expressions even say 'creating the obedience itself.' These two statements are based on the two interpretations of the power of obedience discussed earlier (soundness of causes vs. the accident). Then the commentator draws an important implication: the fact that scholars only exclude the disbeliever from being 'given success' (muwaffaq) implies that a believing sinner (muʾmin ʿāṣī) is still considered given success. This is the correct view, contrary to those who say that the one given success cannot sin because he has no power over disobedience. The commentator clarifies by saying that the one given success does not sin with respect to the particular act of obedience he was enabled for, but he may sin in another matter. Similarly, the one abandoned (makhdhūl) does not obey with respect to the act he was abandoned in. To support this, the commentator quotes the famous Sufi al-Junayd, who, when asked if a saint (walī) sins, responded by bowing his head and then reciting the Quranic verse: 'And the command of God is a decree determined' (al-Aḥzāb 33:38), implying that sin happens by divine decree and does not negate sainthood. Then he quotes a poetic line from Ibn al-Fāriḍ: 'Who is it that has never done evil? And who only has the good?' – meaning no one is perfect, and saints too can sin. This passage thus defends the doctrine that believers, even if they sin, can still be recipients of divine enabling in other acts.

Key Terms
توفيق
قولان
قدرة الطاعة
الموفق
المخذول
الولي
الجنيد
ابن الفارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being given success (tawfiq) means one never sins.
Correction: Tawfiq is specific to particular acts; a believer may sin in one area but still be given success in others. The commentator states: 'the one given success does not sin with respect to that in which he was given success.'

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the commentator draw from the fact that scholars only exclude the disbeliever from being muwaffaq?
A) The believing sinner is not given success.
B) The believing sinner is given success, contrary to the view that the muwaffaq cannot sin.
C) The muwaffaq cannot sin because he has no power over disobedience.
D) Both the believer and disbeliever are given success equally.

Key Takeaway
Even believers who sin can be given divine success (tawfiq) in other acts, because tawfiq is specific to each act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have sinned, do you truly believe that God can still enable you to obey Him in other areas, or do you feel completely abandoned? How does the act-specific nature of tawfiq challenge your despair?
?
Think of a person you know who disobeys in one area but obeys in another. Do you view them as entirely abandoned, or can you see the tawfiq in their life? What would change in your heart if you saw them as muwaffaq in some acts?
?
If you claim to be a believer striving for sainthood, are you willing to admit that you still sin? How does the story of al-Junayd's silence and then his Quranic response confront your pride about your own obedience?

The Definition of 'Abd' and Its Inclusive Scope
Author defines 'abd and gives examples including inanimate objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'abd (servant) in Islamic theology refers to any creature that performs voluntary acts, including inanimate objects.

Core Idea
'Abd includes all creatures with choice and action, even trees and pebbles.

The passage begins by defining one of the most important terms in the discussion of human acts: 'abd. The author clarifies that this term is not limited to humans but encompasses any creature that issues a voluntary act. This expansive definition sets the stage for the debate on acquisition (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice that the definition is very concise.
2
Identify the definition of 'abd
Find the exact phrase that defines 'abd. The author says it means every creature from whom a voluntary act originates.
The term 'abd is broader than just humans.
3
Note the examples given
The author gives three examples: the yearning of the tree stump, the walking of a tree, and the glorification of pebbles. These show that non-human objects can perform voluntary acts.
These examples are meant to stretch your understanding.
4
Reflect on the inclusive scope
Think about why the author would include such examples. It emphasizes that 'abd covers any entity capable of voluntary action, not just rational beings.
This broad definition becomes relevant when discussing kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لِلْعَبْدِ) المراد به كل مخلوق يصدر عنه فعل اختياري، قال المصنف : فيشمل حنين الجذع ، ومشي الشجر ، وتسبيحَ الحصى. انتهى (١) في (ط): زيادة (بالمقدور). (٢) انظر هداية المريد» (٥٨٠/١). وقد ذكر الماتريدي في التوحيد» (ص) (۳۱۹) أن الجبرية أرجعوا جميع الأفعال إلى الله، ولم يُثبتوا للعباد في التحقيق فعلاً . وقال العلامة الشعراني في اليواقيت : العبد بين طرفي الاضطرار، مضطر على الاختيار)، وقال أيضاً : قال الشيخ الأشعري : تعلق القدرة الحادثة كتعلق العلم بالمعلوم من عدم التأثير). وقد جعل القاضي الكسب حالاً؛ لأنه لا فرق بين الحركتين إلا بحال هي تفرقة موجودة في النفس . والذي قال بتأثير القدرة الحادثة كالجويني أسند أسبابها إلى قدرة الله، وإن لم يثبت للعبد استقلالاً، فقد صرح الجويني بتأثير القدرة الحادثة من مدارك العقول، وأنها تؤثر في محلها على شرط الاتصال، ومثل بالعبد الذي ماله بإذن سيده في البيع . ثم موضوع الخلاف بين أهل السنة والمعتزلة هو أفعال العباد التي تدخل تحت قدرتهم الظاهرة، وما سوى ذلك فلا خلاف عند المعتزلة يكونه من عند الله استقلالاً. انظر «شرح المقاصد».
His saying: (for the servant) means every creature from whom a voluntary act originates. The author said: it includes the yearning of the tree stump, the walking of the tree, and the glorification of pebbles. End. (1) In (T): addition: 'by the act of power'. (2) See Hidayat al-Murid, 1/580. And al-Maturidi mentioned in al-Tawhid (p. 319) that the Jabriyya referred all acts to God and did not affirm for servants any actual act. And al-Sha'rani said in al-Yawaqit: 'The servant is between two extremes of necessity, compelled in choice.' And he also said: 'Al-Ash'ari said: The attachment of the originated power is like the attachment of knowledge to the known in terms of lack of effect.' And the judge (al-Qadi) made acquisition a mode (hal), because there is no difference between two movements except by a mode which is a distinguishing feature existing in the soul. And whoever said that the originated power has an effect, like al-Juwayni, attributed its causes to God's power, even if he did not affirm independence for the servant. For al-Juwayni explicitly stated that the originated power has an effect from the perspective of the intellect, and that it affects its locus on condition of connection, and he analogized it to a servant whose property is with his master's permission in selling. Then the subject of disagreement between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila is the acts of servants that fall under their apparent power. What is other than that, there is no disagreement among the Mu'tazila that it is from God independently. See Sharh al-Maqasid.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'abd' (servant) as any creature from whom a voluntary act originates. The author notes that this includes even inanimate objects like the yearning tree stump, the walking tree, and the glorification of pebbles. Then it presents additional scholarly notes: al-Maturidi says the Jabriyya denied any actual act to servants; al-Sha'rani quotes al-Ash'ari that the originated power is non-effective like knowledge; al-Qadi makes acquisition a mode; al-Juwayni affirms some effect of the originated power but under God's authority. Finally, it clarifies that the disagreement with the Mu'tazila is only about acts under the servant's apparent power.

Key Terms
العبد
الفعل الاختياري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'abd refers only to human beings.
Correction: The author explicitly includes all creatures with voluntary action, as seen in the examples of tree stump, tree, and pebbles.

Checkpoint

A) Only prophets
B) Only humans
C) All creatures with voluntary acts
D) Only believers

Key Takeaway
The term 'abd encompasses every creature capable of voluntary action, not just humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even a tree stump and pebbles are considered 'abd because they perform voluntary acts in obedience to God, how does this challenge your own understanding of what it means to be a servant of God? Are you living with the same level of constant submission that these objects display? What concrete change can you make today to align your voluntary actions more with God's will?
?
The yearning of the tree stump towards the Prophet (saw) is an example of a voluntary act from an inanimate object. Reflect on your own connection to the Prophet: Is your love and yearning for him as tangible and real as that of a piece of wood? What can you do this week to deepen that yearning and make it a driving force in your life?
?
Pebbles glorify God constantly. When was the last time you consciously glorified God not just with your tongue but with your entire being, as the pebbles do? What would change in your daily routine if you saw every voluntary action—even the smallest—as an act of worship, just like the tree's walking or the pebble's praise?

Scholarly Views on Kasb and the Originated Power
Presentation of various scholarly positions on kasb and originated power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing scholarly opinions on the nature of human acquisition (kasb) and the role of the originated power (al-qudra al-haditha).

Core Idea
Scholars differed on whether the originated power has any effect or is merely a locus for God's action.

After defining the subject of discussion, the passage presents a series of views from prominent scholars such as al-Maturidi, al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi, and al-Juwayni. These views revolve around whether the originated power (the power created in the servant) has any causal influence on the act or is simply a mode (hal) without effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify each scholar's view
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the translation. As you read, note the different positions mentioned: al-Maturidi, al-Sha'rani (quoting al-Ash'ari), al-Qadi, and al-Juwayni.
The passage is a collection of opinions; keep track of who said what.
2
Understand the Jabriyya position
Al-Maturidi reports that the Jabriyya deny any actual act to the servant. This is the extreme view on the spectrum.
This is a starting point for the discussion.
3
Analyze al-Ash'ari's analogy
Al-Ash'ari compares the originated power's relation to its act to knowledge's relation to the known—both are non-effective. This denies causal efficacy to the servant's power.
The analogy is crucial for understanding the Ash'ari position.
4
Contrast al-Qadi's mode view and al-Juwayni's effect view
Al-Qadi makes acquisition a mode (hal), a mere distinction. Al-Juwayni, however, affirms that the originated power has an effect, but only as a dependent cause under God's power.
Al-Juwayni's view is closer to a form of occasionalism with secondary causality.
5
Note the limitation of disagreement with the Mu'tazila
The passage concludes that the disagreement with the Mu'tazila is only about acts under the servant's apparent power, not about all acts.
This frames the broader debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction

According to the passage, which scholar compared the originated power's relation to its act to knowledge's relation to the known?

Scholars differed on whether the originated power has any effect, with al-Ash'ari denying it, al-Qadi seeing it as a mode, and al-Juwayni affirming a dependent effect.

?

?
If you truly believed that your originated power has no real effect, would your sense of responsibility before Allah change? Reflect on a specific sin you committed: did you attribute it to your own choice or to Allah's decree?
?
The passage clarifies that the real disagreement with the Mu'tazila is only about acts under your apparent power. Are there areas in your life where you deny either your own accountability or Allah's absolute control? Commit to one concrete way you can align your belief with both.

4 min
Definitions of Good and Evil: Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna
Classifies the technical definitions of 'evil' and 'good' with their examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definitions of 'good' (hasan) and 'evil' (qabih) according to the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna, and see how these definitions categorize actions.

Core Idea
Good and evil are technical terms with precise theological definitions.

In this lesson, we examine how the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna define 'good' (hasan) and 'evil' (qabih). These definitions are foundational for Islamic theology and legal theory. The passage first gives the Mu'tazilite view, then presents a Sunni perspective, and finally clarifies examples from the poem.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general equivalence
Note that 'evil' is expressed as 'al-qabih' and 'good' as 'al-hasan'. This equivalence sets the stage for the detailed definitions.
2
Examine the Mu'tazilite definition
Read how the Mu'tazila define 'qabih' as that which is connected to blame in this world and punishment in the hereafter, specifically the forbidden (haram). Also see how 'hasan' covers all other acts: obligatory, recommended, permissible, disliked, and even contrary to the better.
For them, everything not haram is hasan.
3
Compare with Ahl al-Sunna views
Many Sunni scholars say that what is absolutely prohibited is 'qabih'. However, the best view according to Imam al-Haramayn is that the disliked (makruh) and contrary to the better (khilaf al-awla) are neither hasan nor qabih.
4
Understand the examples from the poem
The author gives 'Islam' as an example of good (khayr) and 'ignorance of disbelief' as an example of evil (sharr). He also explains the literary device of 'laff wa nashr' and the grammatical construction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الشَّرِّ وَالخَيْرِ) اعلمُ أنَّهم يعبرون عن الأول بالقبيح، وعن الثاني بالحسن، واصطلحتِ المعتزلة على أنّ القبيح ما يكون متعلق الذم في العاجل؛ أي: الدنيا والعقاب في الآجل ؛ أي: الآخرة، فيكون القبيح هو الحرام بخصوصه، وعلى الحسن ما لا يكون متعلَّق الذم والعقاب، فيشمل الواجب والمندوب والمباح والمكروه وخلاف الأولى إن لم ندخله في المكروه، فهذه الأمور كلُّها حسنةٌ عندهم . واصطلح كثير من أهل السنة على أنّ المنهي * مطلقاً قبيح، والأحسنُ ما قاله إمام الحرمين : أنَّ المكروه ومنه خلافُ الأولى ليس حسناً ولا قبيحاً . وقوله : (كالإِسْلَام) مثال للخير . وقوله: (وَجَهْلِ الكُفْرِ) مثال للشر، ففيه مع ما قبله لفّ ونشر مشوش، والإضافة ( في جهل الكفر للبيان؛ أي جهل هو الكفر، أو من إضافة السبب للمسبب.
His saying: (evil and good) know that they express the first as 'al-qabih' and the second as 'al-hasan'. The Mu'tazila agreed upon the convention that 'al-qabih' is that which is connected to blame in the immediate (i.e., this world) and punishment in the future (i.e., the hereafter), so 'al-qabih' is specifically the forbidden (haram). And 'al-hasan' is that which is not connected to blame and punishment, so it includes the obligatory, recommended, permissible, disliked, and contrary to the better (khilaf al-awla) if we do not include it in the disliked; so all these matters are 'hasan' according to them. And many of Ahl al-Sunna agreed that what is prohibited absolutely is 'qabih', and the best is what Imam al-Haramayn said: that the disliked and among it the contrary to the better is neither 'hasan' nor 'qabih'. And his saying: (like Islam) is an example of good. And his saying: (and ignorance of disbelief) is an example of evil. So in it is with what precedes an intertwined 'laff wa nashr', and the idafa in 'ignorance of disbelief' is for clarification, i.e., ignorance that is disbelief, or from attribution of cause to effect.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the technical terms 'good' (khayr/hasan) and 'evil' (sharr/qabih). The author first notes the general equivalence: 'evil' is 'qabih', 'good' is 'hasan'. He then gives the Mu'tazilite definition: qabih is what deserves blame in this world and punishment in the next, so it is specifically haram; hasan is what does not deserve blame or punishment, covering wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, and even khilaf al-awla if not included in makruh. Then he gives a Sunni view from many scholars: what is absolutely prohibited (manhi) is qabih. The best view, from Imam al-Haramayn, is that makruh and khilaf al-awla are neither hasan nor qabih. Finally, he explains the examples from the poem: 'Islam' for good, 'ignorance of disbelief' for evil, noting the literary device of 'laff wa nashr' and the grammatical idafa in 'ignorance of disbelief'.

Misconception: The terms 'good' and 'evil' are absolute moral labels independent of theological definition.
Correction: This passage shows that these terms have technical definitions that vary between schools, so they are not absolute but defined by conventions.

Checkpoint

A) Everything disliked
B) The forbidden (haram)
C) Anything subject to blame

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila define 'good' and 'evil' based on consequences (blame/punishment), while Ahl al-Sunna refine the categories further.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How will you start evaluating your actions by the criteria of divine command rather than social approval?
?
Imam al-Haramayn identifies a category of actions that are neither good nor evil—the disliked and the contrary to the better. Is there a habit or practice in your daily life that falls into this grey area? Are you content with it, or does this lesson challenge you to pursue what is better (al-awla) even when it is not required?
?
The Mu'tazila define evil (qabih) strictly as what incurs blame and punishment. Does your own sense of right and wrong ever rely on fear of consequences rather than the intrinsic command of Allah? In what area of your life are you most tempted to base your morality on outcomes rather than obedience? Commit now to one change where you will follow Allah's command regardless of result.

Harmful vs. Harmless Ignorance: The Phrase 'Ignorance of Disbelief'
Explanation of 'ignorance of disbelief' as an example of evil with definition of kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between harmful ignorance (which leads to disbelief) and harmless ignorance (such as ignorance of Allah's essence), and why the poet specifically paired 'ignorance' with 'disbelief'.

Core Idea
Not all ignorance is blameworthy; some ignorance is harmless.

This passage unpacks the poet's phrase 'ignorance of disbelief' as an example of evil. The commentator clarifies a subtle point: while ignorance can cause disbelief, there is a type of ignorance that is not blameworthy—our inability to fully comprehend Allah's essence. This lesson explores that distinction and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the rhetorical term 'laff wa nashr' mentioned in the commentary.
2
Identify the rhetorical device and grammatical analysis
The commentator notes a 'disordered laff wa nashr' between this phrase and the previous example of good. Also, understand the two possible interpretations of the idafa (annexation) in 'ignorance of disbelief'.
3
Understand the definition of disbelief (kufr)
Disbelief is defined as the opposite of faith: denial of what is necessarily known from the messenger. Note the example of throwing a Quran in filth as an act implying denial.
This definition is crucial for distinguishing harmful ignorance from harmless.
4
Grasp the key nuance: harmless ignorance
The poet added 'ignorance' to highlight that some ignorance is not harmful, such as our ignorance of Allah's essence and attributes not indicated by His actions.
This is supported by Abu Bakr al-Siddiq's saying: 'Inability to comprehend is comprehension.'
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this nuance protects against the misconception that all ignorance is blameworthy. It also shows the depth of Islamic theology in distinguishing types of ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (كالإِسْلَام) مثال للخير . وقوله: (وَجَهْلِ الكُفْرِ) مثال للشر، ففيه مع ما قبله لفّ ونشر مشوش، والإضافة ( في جهل الكفر للبيان؛ أي جهل هو الكفر، أو من إضافة السبب للمسبب، فإنَّ الجهل سبب للكفر ، وإن كان له سبب آخر وهو العناد، وقد تقدم تعريف الجهل (1) وانقسامه إلى بسيط ومركب والكفر ضدُّ الإيمان، فهو إنكارُ ما عُلِمَ مجيء الرسول به من الدين بالضرورة، أو ما يستلزم ذلك كإلقاء مصحف في القاذورة، وإنّما أضاف الناظم الجهل إلى الكفر لينبة على أن من الجهل ما لا يضرُّ ، كجهلنا بجلال الله وصفاته التي لم تدل عليها أفعاله، كما يشير إليه قول الصديق الأكبر: (العجز عن الإدراك إدراك ) .
And His saying: (like Islam) is an example of good. And His saying: (and ignorance of disbelief) is an example of evil. In it, along with what precedes it, there is a disordered laff wa nashr. The annexation in 'ignorance of disbelief' is for clarification, i.e., ignorance that is disbelief, or it is an annexation of cause to effect, for ignorance is the cause of disbelief, though it has another cause which is obstinacy. And the definition of ignorance has preceded, and its division into simple and compound. And disbelief is the opposite of faith, so it is denial of what is known to have come from the messenger by necessity of religion, or that which entails that, such as throwing a copy of the Quran into a filthy place. And the author only added ignorance to disbelief to indicate that there is ignorance that does not harm, like our ignorance of the majesty of Allah and His attributes that His actions do not indicate, as is indicated by the saying of the greatest Siddiq: 'Inability to comprehend is comprehension.'
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the phrase 'ignorance of disbelief' (jahli al-kufr) used in the poem as an example of evil. The author notes a rhetorical device called laff wa nashr mushabbash (disordered wrapping and unfolding) between this phrase and the previous example of good (Islam). He then analyzes the grammatical construction: the idafa (annexation) can be understood either as specifying that ignorance is disbelief itself, or as a causal relationship where ignorance causes disbelief. He then defines disbelief (kufr) as the opposite of faith (iman): a denial of what is necessarily known to be from the messenger, or any act that implies such denial, like throwing a Quran in filth. Importantly, the author explains why the poet specifically mentioned ignorance alongside disbelief: to show that not all ignorance is harmful. For example, our ignorance of God's essence and attributes that are not indicated by His actions is not blameworthy—in fact, recognizing our inability to comprehend is itself a form of comprehension (as Abu Bakr al-Siddiq said). This nuance distinguishes between harmful ignorance (which leads to disbelief) and neutral ignorance (which is not blameworthy).

جهل الكفر
الكفر
الجهل البسيط
الجهل المركب
الإيمان
العجز عن الإدراك إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All ignorance is blameworthy.
Correction: The passage clarifies that ignorance of Allah's essence and attributes not indicated by His actions is not harmful, and may even be a form of comprehension.

Checkpoint
Why did the poet add 'ignorance' to 'disbelief' in the phrase 'ignorance of disbelief'?
A) To indicate that all ignorance is disbelief.
B) To indicate that there is ignorance that does not harm, unlike disbelief.
C) To emphasize that disbelief is a form of ignorance.
D) To show that ignorance is worse than disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance that leads to disbelief is harmful, but ignorance of Allah's essence is a form of recognition, as stated by Abu Bakr al-Siddiq.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt frustrated by your inability to fully understand Allah's attributes? Could it be that this very frustration is a form of true knowledge, as Abu Bakr al-Siddiq said? Will you ask Allah to replace that frustration with humble contentment and worship of Him as He has revealed Himself?
?
Is there specific Islamic knowledge you have avoided learning because it feels uncomfortable or challenging, knowing that such ignorance may lead to harmful consequences for your faith? Write down one area you have neglected and commit to studying it starting tomorrow.
?
When you hear the word 'ignorance', do you automatically assume it is negative? Reflect on the passage: some ignorance is harmless. Are there things you have been anxious about not knowing about Allah that He has not commanded you to know? Will you accept your limits and focus on acting on what you do know?

The Meaning of Hawa (Desire) and Its Consequences
Define الهوى and its negative connotation, with Quranic and hadith examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the term 'hawa' (desire) as used in classical Islamic texts, its typical negative connotation, and its relation to sectarian positions on prophethood.

Core Idea
Desire (hawa) is not mere want—it is the inclination that leads one away from truth and into the Fire.

This lesson unpacks the term 'hawa' from the poem. It explains how the word is used to describe false beliefs that Satan beautifies, and how it contrasts with true guidance. It also shows how different groups have either affirmed or denied the sending of prophets, illustrating the consequences of following desire.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text carefully and then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the first part defines hawa, the second part addresses the group's playing with the concept of prophethood.
Pay attention to the footnote markers; they indicate citations.
2
Analyze the definition of hawa
Identify the core meaning of hawa as 'inclination contrary to the truth.' Understand why it is called hawa (because it causes falling into Fire). Also note the exceptional usage in the hadith about Aisha.
Contrast hawa with hawa' (long vowel) which means atmosphere.
3
Examine the Quranic and hadith evidence
The commentator cites the Quranic prohibition 'Do not follow desire' and the hadith about the Prophet's desire being fulfilled by Allah. Recognize that the Quranic usage is the typical negative one, while the hadith shows a praiseworthy desire.
4
Connect to sectarian positions
The second part mentions groups that accepted prophethood (Mu'tazila, philosophers) and those that rejected it (Sumaniyya, Brahmins). Understand that these positions stem from following hawa or rejecting it.
This shows the practical relevance of the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فَدَعْ هَوَى قَوْمٍ) أي: إذا عرفت أن الإرسال من الجائز العقلي في تعالى، وأن الإيمان به واجب .. فدع عنك هوى قوم والمراد بهواهم : مَهْوِيَّهم ؛ وهو ما اعتقدوه من الاعتقادات الباطلة التي زينها الشيطان لهم، والهوى - بالقصر - عند الإطلاق ينصرف إلى الميل إلى خلاف الحقِّ غالباً، نحو: ﴿وَلَا تَتَّبِعِ الْهَوَى [م: ٢٦] ، سمي هوى لأنه يهوي بصاحبه في النار (۲) ، ومن غير الغالب قول السيدة عائشة له : ما أرى ربَّكَ إلا يسارعُ في هواك » (۳) ، وقد يُطلق على مطلق الميل، فيشمل الميل للحق وغيره وأما بالمد فهو ما بين السماء والأرض.
وقوله : (بِهِمْ قَدْ لَعِبَا) بألف الإطلاق؛ أي: قد تلاعب بهم لا بغيرهم حتى أوقعهم في البدع والمعاصي أو الكفر ، فأوجب الإرسال بعضهم كالمعتزلة والحكماء، وأحاله بعضهم كالسمنية والبراهمة.
His saying: (So leave the desire of a people) means: when you know that sending [messengers] is among the rational possibilities with respect to the Most High, and that belief in it is obligatory... then leave aside the desire of a people. And what is meant by their desire: their object of desire; and it is what they have believed of false beliefs which Satan has beautified for them. And 'al-hawa' (desire) – with qasr (short vowel) – when used absolutely, generally refers to inclination contrary to the truth, such as: {And do not follow desire} [Sad 26:?]. It is called hawa because it causes its possessor to fall into the Fire (2). And from the non-general usage is the saying of Lady Aisha to him: 'I do not see your Lord except that He hastens to fulfill your desire' (3). And it may be used for absolute inclination, so it includes inclination to the truth and other than it. As for with madd (long vowel), it is what is between heaven and earth.
And his saying: (with them they have played) with the alif of absolute [form]; i.e., they have toyed with them, not with others, until they led them into innovations, sins, or disbelief. So some [groups] affirmed sending [messengers] like the Mu'tazila and the philosophers, and some denied it like the Sumaniyya and the Brahmins.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of two lines from the poem. First, it defines the word 'hawa' (desire) as used in the phrase 'فَدَعْ هَوَى قَوْمٍ'. The commentator clarifies that the desire here refers to the false beliefs that Satan has made attractive. He explains that the term 'hawa' with short vowel (al-qasr), when used without qualification, usually means inclination away from the truth, citing the Quranic verse 'وَلَا تَتَّبِعِ الْهَوَى' (And do not follow desire). It is called 'hawa' because it causes its follower to 'fall' (yahwi) into the Fire. There is also a non-typical usage, such as in the hadith where Aisha said to the Prophet that Allah hastens to fulfill his desire, which is praiseworthy. With long vowel (al-madd), 'hawa'' means the space between heaven and earth. Second, the commentator explains the line 'بِهِمْ قَدْ لَعِبَا' meaning that certain groups have toyed with the concept of prophethood, either affirming it incorrectly (like the Mu'tazila and philosophers) or denying it (like the Sumaniyya and Brahmins).

مهوى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'hawa' means any desire, whether good or bad.
Correction: In Islamic terminology, 'hawa' (with short vowel) most often refers to blameworthy inclination towards falsehood, not all desires.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Hawa (desire) typically denotes blameworthy inclination away from truth, and its root meaning relates to falling into the Fire.

?
The exception shows that the Prophet's desires were fulfilled by Allah because they were aligned with obedience. Examine your heart: are your deepest desires in line with what pleases Allah, or do they oppose His commands? What will you change to make your desires righteous?

5 min
The Scope of Prophetic Infallibility
Defines prophetic infallibility as preservation from all prohibited acts, including makruh and khilaf al-awla, covering outward and inward, before prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophetic infallibility ('isma) means protection from all prohibited acts, both outward and inward, including acts that are disliked or contrary to the better, and that this protection extends to before prophethood and even childhood.

Core Idea
Prophets are protected from every prohibited act, outward and inward, before and after prophethood.

This lesson examines the definition of prophetic infallibility. The author clarifies that 'isma encompasses not only major sins but also minor sins, acts of dislike (karāhah), and acts 'contrary to the better' (khilāf al-awlā). This comprehensive protection covers both external and internal dimensions, applying from the prophet's earliest years.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the term 'al-amānah' at the centre of the definition.
2
Identify the categories of protection
The passage distinguishes between outward prohibitions (e.g., adultery, lying) and inward prohibitions (e.g., envy, pride). List them.
Outward and inward sins are both included.
3
Note the extension to before prophethood
The passage explicitly states that protection includes the period before prophethood and even childhood. This is a key point.
Infallibility is not limited to after prophethood.
4
Reflect on inclusiveness
Consider why even acts that are khilāf al-awlā are included. This shows the high standard of prophetic purity.
This guards against underestimating the scope of 'isma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النبوات
ما عدا التبليغ، فإن التبليغ خاص بالرسل، وبعضُهم عمَّمه للأنبياء؛ لأنه يجب على النبي أن يُبلغ أنه نبي ليُحترم . قوله : (الَامَانَهُ) بالنقل والدرج للوزن؛ وهي حفظ ظواهرهم وبواطنهم من التلبس بمنهي عنه ولو نهي كراهة أو خلاف الأولى فهم محفوظون ظاهراً من الزنا وشرب الخمر والكذب وغير ذلك من منهيات الظاهر، ومحفوظون باطناً الحسد والكبر والرياء، وغير ذلك من منهيات الباطن والمراد : المنهى عنه ، : ولو صورةً، فيشمل ما قبل النبوة ولو في حال الصغر ولا يقعُ منهم مكروه ولا خلاف الأولى، بل ولا مباح على وجه كونه مكروهاً أو خلاف الأولى أو مباحاً، وإذا وقع صورة ذلك فهو للتشريع؛ فيصير واجباً أو مندوباً (٣) في حقهم، فأفعالهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام دائرة بين الواجب والمندوب (٤)، بل في الأولياء الذين هم أتباعُهم من يصل لمقام تصير حركاته وسكناته طاعات بالنيَّات .
Prophethoods. Apart from delivery (of the message), for delivery is specific to messengers, and some generalized it to prophets; because the prophet must announce that he is a prophet in order to be respected. His saying: (al-amānah) by way of transmission and insertion for the meter; and it is the preservation of their outward and inward from being sullied with that which is prohibited, even if the prohibition is one of dislike (karāhah) or contrary to the better (khilāf al-awla). So they are protected outwardly from adultery, drinking wine, lying, and other outward prohibitions, and protected inwardly from envy, pride, ostentation, and other inward prohibitions. The intended meaning: that which is prohibited, even if formally, so it includes what is before prophethood even in childhood. And no disliked act nor contrary to the better occurs from them, nor even a permitted act in a way that it would be disliked, contrary to the better, or permitted. And if such an act formally occurs, it is for legislation; so it becomes obligatory or recommended (٣) in their regard. Thus their acts, peace and blessings upon them, are limited to the obligatory and the recommended (٤). Rather, among the saints who are their followers, there is one who reaches a station where his movements and stillnesses become acts of obedience through intentions.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of prophetic infallibility ('isma). It begins by noting that delivering the message is specific to messengers, though some extend it to prophets because a prophet must announce his prophethood. The passage then explains the term 'al-amānah' (trust) as preserving prophets' outward and inward from any prohibited act, whether the prohibition is based on dislike (karāhah) or contrary to the better (khilāf al-awla). This protection covers both outward sins (like adultery, drinking wine, lying) and inward sins (like envy, pride, ostentation). It emphasizes that protection extends even before prophethood and includes childhood. The passage states categorically that no makrūh (disliked) act or khilāf al-awlā occurs from them, nor even a mubāḥ (permitted) act in a way that would make it makrūh or khilāf al-awlā. If such an act appears formally, it is for legislation (tashrī'), so it becomes obligatory or recommended for them. Consequently, their actions are always either wājib or mandūb. The passage concludes by noting that even saints (awliyā') who are their followers can reach a station where all their movements become acts of obedience through intentions.

أمانة
نهي كراهة
خلاف الأولى
ظاهر
باطن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets are only protected from major sins after prophethood.
Correction: The passage shows they are protected from all prohibited acts, including minor sins and khilaf al-awla, and this protection begins before prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does prophetic protection (al-amānah) include?
A) Only outward sins after prophethood
B) Only major sins
C) Both outward and inward prohibited acts, including before prophethood
D) Only acts contrary to the better

Key Takeaway
Prophetic infallibility is comprehensive, covering all prohibited acts, both outward and inward, and extends to the entirety of the prophet's life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If prophets avoided even 'contrary to the better' (khilaf al-awla), what does that say about your own tolerance for minor sins or questionable actions? Are you content with avoiding only major sins, or will you elevate your standard starting today?
?
The passage lists inward prohibitions like envy, pride, and ostentation. Which of these do you struggle with most, and what concrete step will you take this week to purify your heart from it?
?
Prophetic protection covers childhood. Knowing this, how will you change how you guard your own children or younger family members from prohibited acts, even small ones? What is one specific action you will take this week?

The Impeccability of Prophetic Actions
Explains that prophets never commit disliked acts; apparent instances are legislative, so their actions are always obligatory or recommended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophets never commit acts that are disliked (makruh) or contrary to the better (khilaf al-awla), and that any apparent occurrence of such is for the purpose of legislation, making their actions always obligatory or recommended.

Core Idea
Prophets are free from even disliked acts; their actions are always divinely guided acts of legislation.

This lesson builds on the definition of infallibility to explore the nature of prophetic actions. The author argues that no disliked act (makruh) or act contrary to the better (khilaf al-awla) can ever occur from a prophet. If an action appears to be of that type, it is actually a legislative act that becomes obligatory or recommended. Consequently, the actions of prophets are always confined to the categories of wajib (obligatory) and mandub (recommended).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation to understand the logical flow.
Look for the key negation: 'لا يقع منهم مكروه ولا خلاف الأولى'.
2
Understand the negation
The passage states that no makruh or khilaf al-awla occurs from prophets. This is a strong claim.
It also says no mubah occurs in a way that would be makruh or khilaf al-awla.
3
Grasp the legislative principle
If such an act appears, it is for legislation (tashri'), so it becomes wajib or mandub. This explains how prophets can do acts that look like makruh.
The act's ruling changes in their case.
4
Recognize the consequence
Thus, prophetic actions are always either wajib or mandub. They have no room for mubah or makruh.
This is the conclusion of the argument.
5
Consider the extension to saints
The passage ends by noting that even saints can reach a station where all their actions become obedience through intentions.
This shows the high rank of prophetic followers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النبوات
ما عدا التبليغ، فإن التبليغ خاص بالرسل، وبعضُهم عمَّمه للأنبياء؛ لأنه يجب على النبي أن يُبلغ أنه نبي ليُحترم . قوله : (الَامَانَهُ) بالنقل والدرج للوزن؛ وهي حفظ ظواهرهم وبواطنهم من التلبس بمنهي عنه ولو نهي كراهة أو خلاف الأولى فهم محفوظون ظاهراً من الزنا وشرب الخمر والكذب وغير ذلك من منهيات الظاهر، ومحفوظون باطناً الحسد والكبر والرياء، وغير ذلك من منهيات الباطن والمراد : المنهى عنه ، : ولو صورةً، فيشمل ما قبل النبوة ولو في حال الصغر ولا يقعُ منهم مكروه ولا خلاف الأولى، بل ولا مباح على وجه كونه مكروهاً أو خلاف الأولى أو مباحاً، وإذا وقع صورة ذلك فهو للتشريع؛ فيصير واجباً أو مندوباً (٣) في حقهم، فأفعالهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام دائرة بين الواجب والمندوب (٤)، بل في الأولياء الذين هم أتباعُهم من يصل لمقام تصير حركاته وسكناته طاعات بالنيَّات .
Prophethoods. Apart from delivery (of the message), for delivery is specific to messengers, and some generalized it to prophets; because the prophet must announce that he is a prophet in order to be respected. His saying: (al-amānah) by way of transmission and insertion for the meter; and it is the preservation of their outward and inward from being sullied with that which is prohibited, even if the prohibition is one of dislike (karāhah) or contrary to the better (khilāf al-awla). So they are protected outwardly from adultery, drinking wine, lying, and other outward prohibitions, and protected inwardly from envy, pride, ostentation, and other inward prohibitions. The intended meaning: that which is prohibited, even if formally, so it includes what is before prophethood even in childhood. And no disliked act nor contrary to the better occurs from them, nor even a permitted act in a way that it would be disliked, contrary to the better, or permitted. And if such an act formally occurs, it is for legislation; so it becomes obligatory or recommended (٣) in their regard. Thus their acts, peace and blessings upon them, are limited to the obligatory and the recommended (٤). Rather, among the saints who are their followers, there is one who reaches a station where his movements and stillnesses become acts of obedience through intentions.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of prophetic infallibility ('isma). It begins by noting that delivering the message is specific to messengers, though some extend it to prophets because a prophet must announce his prophethood. The passage then explains the term 'al-amānah' (trust) as preserving prophets' outward and inward from any prohibited act, whether the prohibition is based on dislike (karāhah) or contrary to the better (khilāf al-awla). This protection covers both outward sins (like adultery, drinking wine, lying) and inward sins (like envy, pride, ostentation). It emphasizes that protection extends even before prophethood and includes childhood. The passage states categorically that no makrūh (disliked) act or khilāf al-awlā occurs from them, nor even a mubāḥ (permitted) act in a way that would make it makrūh or khilāf al-awlā. If such an act appears formally, it is for legislation (tashrī'), so it becomes obligatory or recommended for them. Consequently, their actions are always either wājib or mandūb. The passage concludes by noting that even saints (awliyā') who are their followers can reach a station where all their movements become acts of obedience through intentions.

The Two Testimonies Encompass All Beliefs
Commentator explains how the two testimonies unite beliefs and rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two testimonies of Islam encapsulate all the necessary beliefs about Allah and the prophets, including the categories of rational judgment.

Core Idea
The two testimonies are a summary of all Islamic theology.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid explains that the two testimonies (shahādatān) are not just ritual words but encompass all the beliefs of tawhid. The commentator shows how these simple phrases include everything from the obligations, impossibilities, and possibilities concerning Allah and His messengers. By understanding this, we see the Quranic and Prophetic foundation of all kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a matn line and then expands it.
2
Identify the three rational categories
The passage mentions 'wajib, mustahil, ja'iz' for Allah and for the messengers. Find these terms and note how they are applied to both divinity and prophethood.
These three categories are the foundation of kalam reasoning.
3
Understand the grammatical structure
The commentator explains that 'jāmi‘' is a subject (mubtada') and 'shahādānā al-islām' is its agent (fā‘il) that substitutes for the predicate. This grammatical point emphasizes the gathering function of the two testimonies.
Grammar is used here to highlight meaning.
4
Grasp the definition of 'meaning'
The commentator defines ‘meaning’ (ma‘nā) as what is intended by the word, and distinguishes between concept (mafhūm) and denotation (madlūl). This technical vocabulary is key for later discussions.
This shows the precision of Islamic theological language.
5
Reflect on the completeness of the shahādatān
Consider how the two testimonies (Lā ilāha illallāh, Muḥammadur Rasūlullāh) sum up all beliefs about Allah and the Prophet. The commentator says they include all aqā‘id īmāniyya related to ulūhiyya and nubuwwa.
This helps you appreciate the depth of these simple phrases.

الشهادتان تجمعان عقائد التوحيد وأقسام الحكم العقلي. ٦٢ - وَجَامِعٌ مَعْنَى الذِي تَفَرَّرًا شَهادَنَا الإِسْلَامِ فَاطْرَحِ المِرَا قوله : (وَجَامِعٌ ... إلخ) لما فصَّل ما يجب الله وما يستحيل وما يجوز، وما يجب للرسل وما يستحيل وما يجوز . . ذكر ما يتضمن ذلك، و(جامع) مبتدأ لاعتماده على موصوف محذوف والتقدير وشيءٌ جامع، و(شهادنا الإسلام) فاعل سد مسد الخبر. وقوله: (مَعْنَى الذِي تَقَرَّرا) بألف الإطلاق؛ أي معنى هو الذي تقرر في ذهن السامع، فالإضافة للبيان، ويصح أن تكون الإضافة حقيقية؛ أي: معنى ما تقرر من العقائد الألفاظ في موضعه المخصوص من الكتاب وعلى كل فذلك المعنى هو جميع الإيمانية، مما يرجع إلى الألوهية والنبوة وجوباً وجوازاً واستحالة. والمعنى ما يُعنى من اللفظ ، ويُسمى مفهوماً باعتبار كونه يُفهم منه، ومدلولاً باعتبار كون اللفظ يدل عليه . وقوله : (شَهادَنا الإِسْلَام) أي : الشهادتان الدالتان على الإسلام؛ الذي هو الانقياد الظاهري كما تقدَّم، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة الدال للمدلول، أو اللتان هما سبب
And the two testimonies gather the meanings that have been established: the two testimonies of Islam, so abandon disputation. His saying: (And gatherer... etc.) When he detailed what is wajib for Allah, what is mustahil, and what is ja'iz, and what is wajib for the messengers, what is mustahil, and what is ja'iz ... he mentioned what encompasses that. And (gatherer) is a mubtada' because it depends on a deleted described noun, the estimation is 'a thing that gathers', and (the two testimonies of Islam) is the subject that takes the place of the predicate. And his saying: (the meaning of that which has been established) with the alif of release, i.e., a meaning that has been established in the mind of the hearer. So the idāfa is for clarification, and it is possible that the idāfa is real, i.e., the meaning of what has been established of beliefs – the words in their specific place in the book. In any case, that meaning is all of the beliefs of faith, which revert to divinity (ulūhiyya) and prophethood (nubuwwa) in terms of obligation, permission, and impossibility. And meaning is what is intended from the word, and is called a concept (mafhūm) in terms of being understood from it, and a denotation (madlūl) in terms of the word indicating it. And his saying: (the two testimonies of Islam) i.e., the two testimonies that indicate Islam, which is outward submission as previously mentioned. So the idāfa in his speech is of the indicator to the indicated, or they are the cause...
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new section on the two testimonies (shahādatān) that form the foundation of Islam. The commentator explains the line from the matn: 'Wa jāmi' ma'nā alladhī taqarrarā shahādānā al-islām.' He states that after detailing the attributes of Allah and the prophets (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz), the poet now mentions something that summarizes all of that: the two testimonies. The commentator performs a grammatical analysis: 'jāmi' is a subject (mubtada') that depends on an omitted described noun (like 'shay' jāmi' '), and 'shahādānā al-islām' is the subject (fā'il) that substitutes for the predicate. Then he explains 'ma'nā alladhī taqarrarā': 'the meaning of that which has been established' in the mind of the listener. The idāfa (construct) can be explanatory or real. That meaning encompasses all beliefs of faith (aqā'id īmāniyya) related to divinity and prophethood in terms of obligations (wujūb), permissions (jawāz), and impossibilities (istihāla). Then he defines 'meaning' (ma'nā) as what is intended by the word, also called 'concept' (mafhūm) from the perspective of understanding, and 'denotation' (madlūl) from the perspective of indication. Finally, he explains 'shahādānā al-islām' as the two testimonies that indicate Islam, which is outward submission (inqiyād ẓāhirī). The idāfa is from the indicator to the indicated. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that the explanation continues.

The Two Testimonies Encompass All Beliefs
Commentary on a matn verse explaining the comprehensiveness of the shahadas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two testimonies of Islam encompass all beliefs of Tawheed through their meanings, and why Shaykh al-Sanusi emphasized the entailment interpretation.

Core Idea
The meaning of the two testimonies, not their words, contains all Islamic beliefs.

In this passage, the commentator elaborates on a verse from the matn stating that the two testimonies are comprehensive of all beliefs. He explains that this comprehensiveness is by entailment, not by wording, and clarifies the relationship between the real meaning of the shahada and its logical implications. This distinction is crucial for understanding the structure of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the main claim that the two testimonies encompass all beliefs.
The passage includes grammatical analysis and multiple interpretations.
2
Note the three interpretations of 'shahadana al-Islam'
Identify the three possible relationships between the shahada and Islam: indicator, cause, and part. Understand each interpretation.
This shows the flexibility of the phrase.
3
Understand comprehensiveness by entailment
Recognize that the comprehensiveness is through the meaning, not the wording, and that it works by entailment (luzum). The implicans (malzum) is described as comprising its implications.
4
Analyze the first shahada
Study how 'la ilaha illa Allah' necessarily implies God's self-sufficiency and everything else's neediness. The real meaning and the entailment interpretation are compared.
Al-Sanusi chose the entailment for teaching because it connects more clearly to previous beliefs.
5
Reflect on the command to abandon disputation
Consider the verse's call to 'abandon disputation' after understanding this comprehensiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشهادتان تجمعان عقائد التوحيد وأقسام الحكم العقلي
٦٢ - وَجَامِعٌ مَعْنَى الذِي تَفَرَّرًا شَهادَنَا الإِسْلَامِ فَاطْرَحِ المِرَا 
قوله : (وَجَامِعٌ ... إلخ) لما فصَّل ما يجب الله وما يستحيل وما يجوز، وما يجب للرسل وما يستحيل وما يجوز . . ذكر ما يتضمن ذلك، و(جامع) مبتدأ لاعتماده(۲) على موصوف محذوف والتقدير وشيءٌ جامع، و(شهادنا الإسلام) فاعل سد مسد الخبر. وقوله: (مَعْنَى الذِي تَقَرَّرا) بألف الإطلاق؛ أي معنى هو الذي تقرر في ذهن السامع، فالإضافة للبيان، ويصح أن تكون الإضافة حقيقية؛ أي: معنى ما تقرر من العقائد الألفاظ في موضعه المخصوص من الكتاب وعلى كل فذلك المعنى هو جميع الإيمانية، مما يرجع إلى الألوهية والنبوة وجوباً وجوازاً واستحالة. والمعنى ما يُعنى من اللفظ ، ويُسمى مفهوماً باعتبار كونه يُفهم منه، ومدلولاً باعتبار كون اللفظ يدل عليه . وقوله : (شَهادَنا الإِسْلَام) أي : الشهادتان الدالتان على الإسلام؛ الذي هو الانقياد الظاهري كما تقدَّم، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة الدال للمدلول، أو اللتان هما سبب في الإسلام، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة السبب للمسبب، أو اللتان هما الجزء الأعظم من مُسمَّى الإسلام، بناءً على أنه الهيئة المركبة من الأركان الخمسة المذكورة في حديث: «بني الإسلام على خمس ، فالإضافة في كلامه من إضافة الجزء للكل (۲ ) . والجامع لما تقدَّم من العقائد إنَّما هو معنى الشهادتين لا لفظهما ، فكلام المصنف على حذف مضاف ؛ أي معنى شهادتي الإسلام كما أشار إليه الشارح، ومعنى جمعه لها استلزامه لها ؛ لأن الملزوم يصح وصفه بجمعه للوازمه بالنظر لدلالته عليه وقوله : (فَاطْرَح المِرَا) تكملةٌ ؛ أي: إذا علمت أن كلمتي الشهادتين جمعتا جميع ما تقرر من العقائد الإيمانية، فاترك الجدال في صحة جمعهما لما ذكر . وبيان ما ذكره أن الجملة الأولى نفتِ الألوهية عن غيره تعالى، وأثبتتها له، وحقيقة الألوهية العبادة بحقِّ، ويلزم منها استغناء الإله عن كل ما سواه، وافتقار كلّ ما عداه إليه، فحقيقة الإله المعبود بحقِّ، ويلزم منه أنه مُستغن عن كل ما سواه، ومفتقر إليه كلُّ ما عداه فمعنى (لا إله إلا الله الحقيقي : لا معبود بحق في الواقع إلا الله، ومعناها بطريق اللزوم لا مُستغنياً عن كلِّ ما سواه، ومُفتقراً إليه كلُّ ما عداه إلا الله ، فتفسير الشيخ السنوسي الذي ذكره في الصغرى باللازم لا بالحقيقة. وإنما اختاره لكون استلزامه للعقائد المتقدّمة أظهر من استلزام المعنى الحقيقي لها .
The two testimonies combine the beliefs of tawhid and the divisions of rational judgment. 62 - And comprehensive of the meaning which has been established are our two testimonies of Islam, so abandon disputation. His saying: (And comprehensive ... etc.) When he detailed what is necessary for God, what is impossible, and what is possible, and what is necessary for the messengers, what is impossible, and what is possible, he mentioned what includes that. (Comprehensive) is a mubtada' due to its dependence on a deleted described thing, the estimation being 'a comprehensive thing', and (our two testimonies of Islam) is the subject that takes the place of the predicate. His saying: (the meaning which has been established) with an absolute alif; i.e., a meaning that has been established in the mind of the listener, so the idafa is for explanation, and it is possible that the idafa is real; i.e., the meaning of what has been established of the beliefs in their specific place in the book. In any case, that meaning is all of the beliefs of faith, relating to divinity and prophethood in terms of obligation, possibility, and impossibility. 'Meaning' is what is intended from the utterance, and it is called a concept (mafhum) in terms of being understood from it, and a signification (madlul) in terms of the utterance indicating it. His saying: (our two testimonies of Islam) i.e., the two testimonies that indicate Islam, which is outward submission as previously; so the idafa in his speech is from adding the indicator to the indicated, or they are the cause in Islam, so the idafa is from adding the cause to the effect, or they are the greatest part of the name 'Islam', based on it being the composite state of the five pillars mentioned in the hadith: 'Islam is built on five', so the idafa is from adding the part to the whole. And the comprehensive of the previous beliefs is only the meaning of the two testimonies, not their wording. So the author's speech is with a deleted addendum; i.e., the meaning of the two testimonies of Islam as the commentator indicated. And the meaning of their comprehensiveness is their entailment of them, because the implicans (malzum) is correctly described as comprising its implications (lawazim) in view of its indication of them. And his saying: (abandon disputation) is a completion; i.e., if you know that the two words of the testimonies have combined all the established beliefs of faith, then leave argumentation about the correctness of their comprehensiveness of what was mentioned. And the explanation of what he mentioned is that the first sentence negated divinity from other than Him, may He be exalted, and affirmed it for Him. The reality of divinity is rightful worship, and it necessarily follows from it the self-sufficiency of the deity from everything else, and the neediness of everything else towards Him. So the reality of 'al-ilah' is the one worshipped rightly, and it necessarily follows from it that He is self-sufficient from everything else, and everything else is in need of Him. So the real meaning of 'la ilaha illa Allah' is: there is none worshipped by right in reality except Allah. And its meaning by way of entailment (luzum) is: there is none self-sufficient from everything else, and everything else in need of Him, except Allah. So the interpretation of Shaykh al-Sanusi which he mentioned in al-Sughra is by entailment not by the real meaning. And he only chose it because its entailment of the previous beliefs is more apparent than the entailment of the real meaning for them.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is an exegetical definition and proof. It explains the verse 'وجامع معنى الذي تقررا شهادنا الإسلام فاطرح المرا' from the matn. The commentary begins by noting the context: the author has previously detailed what is necessary, impossible, and possible for God and for the messengers. The word 'جامع' (comprehensive) is analyzed grammatically as a mubtada' (subject) depending on a deleted described thing, with 'شهادنا الإسلام' as its predicate. The phrase 'معنى الذي تقررا' means 'the meaning which has been established' in the listener's mind, and the idafa (construct phrase) is either explicative or real. The established meaning is all the beliefs of faith (al-imaniyya) concerning divinity (uluhiyya) and prophethood (nubuwwa), categorized by necessity (wujub), possibility (jawaz), and impossibility (istihala). The term 'meaning' (ma'na) is defined as what is intended from the utterance, called concept (mafhum) as it is understood, and signification (madlul) as the utterance indicates it. Then the phrase 'شهادنا الإسلام' is interpreted in three ways: (1) the two testimonies indicate Islam (outward submission), so the idafa is of indicator to indicated; (2) they are the cause of Islam, so idafa of cause to effect; (3) they are the greatest part of the name 'Islam', based on the hadith of the five pillars, so idafa of part to whole. The key point is that the comprehensive (jam') of previous beliefs is the meaning of the two testimonies, not the wording. The author's expression involves a deleted addendum (mudaf mahzuf), i.e., 'the meaning of the two testimonies of Islam'. The comprehensiveness is by entailment (luzum): the implicans (malzum) is described as comprising its implications (lawazim). After establishing this, the verse command 'فاطرح المرا' (abandon disputation) is explained: once you know the two testimonies encompass all established beliefs, leave argumentation about their comprehensiveness. The passage then explains the first testimony 'لا إله إلا الله': its real meaning is 'none worshipped by right except Allah'. But from this meaning necessarily follow self-sufficiency (istighna') of God and neediness (iftiqar) of all else. The interpretation of Shaykh al-Sanusi in his al-Sughra uses the necessary entailment rather than the real meaning because it makes the connection to the previous beliefs more apparent.

Key Terms

شهادتان
الإسلام
المعنى

المدلول
الإضافة
الملزوم
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The words of the two testimonies themselves encompass all beliefs.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is the meaning of the testimonies that is comprehensive, not the wording. The comprehensiveness is by entailment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Shaykh al-Sanusi interpret 'la ilaha illa Allah' by entailment rather than by its real meaning?
A) Because the real meaning is false
B) Because the entailment shows the connection to previous beliefs more clearly
C) Because the real meaning is not understood
D) Because he rejected the real meaning

Key Takeaway
The two testimonies of Islam, through their meanings, entail all the beliefs concerning divinity and prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson teaches that the shahada implies Allah's self-sufficiency and your complete neediness. In what area of your life do you still act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you really internalized your need for Allah in that area?
?
The author commands 'abandon disputation' once you know the shahada encompasses all beliefs. Is there a theological issue you keep debating or doubting that this lesson should have settled for you? What would it cost you to stop disputing and submit?
