The Quran as a Miracle Rendering All Incapable
Defines the Quran's miraculous nature as rendering all creation incapable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'mu'jiz al-bashar' and why the Quran's challenge to produce its like applies to all creation, with special mention of humans and jinn.

Core Idea
The Quran leaves all creatures helpless to produce its like.

This lesson explores a key attribute of the Quran: its miraculous nature that renders every created being incapable of producing anything similar. The author defines the term 'mu'jiz al-bashar' and supports it with a verse from Surah al-Isra. We will see why humans and jinn are specifically mentioned and how this miracle relates to divine power.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'mu'jiz al-bashar'
Read the Arabic phrase and its translation. Note that it means 'making humans incapable' – but the author clarifies that all creatures are included.
The term focuses on humans because they actively opposed the Quran.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
Read verse 17:88 carefully. Notice the challenge to both humans and jinn to produce something like the Quran, with the emphatic negation 'la ya'tuna bi-mithlihi'.
This verse is the main evidence for the Quran's inimitability.
3
Understand why humans and jinn are specified
The passage explains that they are mentioned because opposition can be conceived from them, unlike angels who are infallible. The poet restricts to humans because they actually attempted opposition.
This shows the precision of the Quran's language and the author's commentary.
4
Learn the etymology of 'bashar'
The word 'bashar' is derived from 'bishra' meaning skin, because the skin is apparent. This is a linguistic note.
This adds depth to the understanding of the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (معجز البشر) أي: مصيرهم عاجزين عن معارضته، والإتيان بمثله، بل كل المخلوقات كذلك إجماعا. و{قُلْ لَئِنِ اجْتَمَعَتِ الْإِنْسُ وَالْجِنُّ عَلَى أَنْ يَأْتُوا بِمِثْلِ هَذَا الْقُرْآنِ لَا يَأْتُونَ بِمِثْلِهِ وَلَوْ كَانَ بَعْضُهُمْ لِبَعْضٍ ظَهِيرًا} [الإسراء: 88] أي: معينا. وخص الإنس والجن مع أن سائر المخلوقات كذلك؛ لأنهما اللذان يتصور منهما المعارضة، بخلاف غيرهما كالملائكة لعصمتهم. واقتصار الناظم على البشر؛ لأنهم الذين تصدوا لذلك بالفعل. والبشر هم بنو آدم، سموا بذلك لبدو بشرتهم التي هي ظاهر الجلد.
His saying: 'The miracle of humans (mu'jiz al-bashar)' means: making them incapable of opposing it and producing its like; rather, all creatures are [incapable] likewise by consensus. And {Say: If mankind and jinn gathered together to produce the like of this Quran, they could not produce the like thereof, even if they were helpers of one another} [al-Isra: 88], i.e., assistants. And He specified humans and jinn although all other creatures are likewise, because they are the ones from whom opposition can be conceived, unlike others such as the angels due to their infallibility. And the author restricted it to humans because they are the ones who actually undertook opposition. And 'humans' (al-bashar) are the children of Adam, so named because of the appearance (buduww) of their skin (bishra) which is the apparent part of the skin.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mu'jiz al-bashar' (miracle that makes humans incapable) as used by the poet. It means that the Quran causes all of creation to be incapable of producing its like, and this is a consensus. The proof is the Quranic verse challenging humans and jinn to produce something similar, and even with mutual assistance they cannot. The verse specifically mentions humans and jinn because they are the ones who could potentially oppose, unlike angels who are infallible. The poet's restriction to humans is because they actually attempted opposition. The passage also gives the etymology of 'bashar': they are called that because their skin (bishra) is apparent.

Key Terms
معجز البشر
الإعجاز
القرآن
الإسراء: 88

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The incapacity is due to a lack of ability in creatures.
Correction: The incapacity is due to divine prevention or inherent inability, not merely lack of skill. The verse shows it is an absolute inability.

Checkpoint
Why does the verse in Surah al-Isra specifically mention humans and jinn and not all creatures?
A) Because only humans and jinn are addressed by the Quran.
B) Because they are the ones from whom opposition can be conceived.
C) Because angels attempted opposition.
D) Because all creatures are incapable, but humans and jinn are the most eloquent.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is a miracle that renders all creatures, especially humans and jinn, absolutely incapable of producing its like, as proven by divine challenge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you read the Quran as a challenge to your intellect and ability, or as a call to submission? When you hear the verse telling humans and jinn they cannot produce its like, do you feel humbled or defensive? What might change in your daily engagement with the Quran if you truly believed you are helpless before it?
?
The poet restricts 'mu'jiz al-bashar' to humans because they actually attempted opposition. In what ways do you subtly oppose the Quran's authority—by ignoring its commands, rationalizing disobedience, or preferring your own judgment? What would full surrender to its miraculous nature require from you today?
?
The word 'bashar' links your humanity to your skin, the most apparent part of you. When you see your own skin, does it remind you that you are created and thus incapable of matching the Quran's words? How can that physical reminder transform the way you hold, recite, and live by the Quran?

The Companions are the Best Generation
Sanusi's commentator explains that the Companions are the best generation after prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Companions are considered the best generation after the prophets and how their ranks differ based on closeness to the Prophet and sacrifice.

Core Idea
The Companions are the best of all people except prophets, with an internal hierarchy based on merit.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed explains a line of the poem that declares the Companions the best of all centuries. The commentator clarifies that this applies except for prophets and messengers, provides two hadith proofs, and adds a nuanced point about differing ranks among the Companions themselves. As you engage with the source, notice how the commentator defends the special status of the Companions while acknowledging distinctions within them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: 'خير القرون' and 'شرف الصحبة'.
The passage is dense with hadith references.
2
Identify the main claim and its exception
The commentator states that the Companions are the best of all centuries, with one exception. Find that exception and understand why it is made.
Prophets and messengers are excluded from this comparison.
3
Examine the two hadith proofs
Two hadiths are cited. Read them carefully. What do they establish about the Companions' status? How do they support the claim?
The first hadith is about God choosing the Companions over the worlds except prophets; the second warns against mistreating them and emphasizes their unparalleled merit.
4
Understand the internal hierarchy among Companions
The commentator adds a concluding remark: not all Companions are equal in rank. Those who accompanied, fought, and died with the Prophet are superior. Reflect on how this nuance fits with the overall claim.
This guards against the misconception that all Companions are identical in virtue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وصَحْبُهُ خَيْرُ القُرُونِ) أي : وأصحابه أفضل القرون المتأخرة والمتقدمة ما عدا الأنبياء والرسل ؛ لحديث: إنَّ الله اختار أصحابي على العالمين سوى النبيين (1) والمرسلين» (١) ، ولحديث: «الله الله في أصحابي لا تتخذوهم غَرَضاً من بعدي، فوالذي نفسي بيده لو أنفق أحدُكم مثلَ أُحدٍ ذهباً ما بلغَ مُدَّ أحدهم ولا نَصِيْفَهُ (۲) ، ولا يخفى ترجيحُ رُتبة مَنْ لازمه ، وقاتل معه، وقتل تحت رايته على من لم يكن كذلك، وإن كان شرف الصحبة حاصلاً للجميع.
His saying: (And his companions are the best of the centuries), meaning: his companions, may God be pleased with them, are the best of the later and earlier centuries except the prophets and messengers, due to the hadith: 'Indeed God chose my companions over the worlds besides the prophets and messengers,' and due to the hadith: 'God, God concerning my companions; do not take them as targets after me. For by the One in whose hand is my soul, if one of you were to spend the equivalent of Mount Uhud in gold, he would not reach the mudd of one of them nor half of it.' And it is not hidden that the rank of one who accompanied him, fought with him, and was killed under his banner is preferred over one who did not do so, even though the honor of companionship is attained by all.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the line 'وَصَحْبُهُ خَيْرُ الْقُرُونِ' from the poem. The commentator first clarifies that the phrase means the Companions are the best of all centuries, past and future, except for prophets and messengers. He then provides two hadith proofs: the first, from al-Bazzar, states that God chose the Companions over all worlds except prophets; the second, from al-Tirmidhi, warns against mistreating them and asserts that even spending gold equal to Mount Uhud cannot match the merit of a Companion's simple measure of charity. The commentator then adds a nuanced point: there is a difference in rank among the Companions themselves—those who accompanied the Prophet closely, fought with him, and died under his banner are superior to those who only had a brief meeting, though all share the honor of companionship.

Key Terms

خير القرون
الأنبياء والرسل
شرف الصحبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Companions are equal in rank and virtue.
Correction: While all Companions share the honor of companionship, those who were closest to the Prophet, fought with him, and were martyred under his command are greater in rank than those who only met him briefly or did not participate in major sacrifices.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the Companions compared to all other generations?
A) They are the best except for prophets and messengers
B) They are equal to prophets
C) They are the best including prophets
D) They are less than later generations

Key Takeaway
The Companions are the best of all centuries except the prophets, and among them, those who made greater sacrifices hold higher rank.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The hadith says even spending gold like Mount Uhud cannot match the charity of a Companion. How does that strike your heart when you consider your own good deeds? Do you rely on your actions alone, or do you humbly seek to follow the example of those whom God chose?
?
Among the Companions, those who fought and died under the Prophet's banner are of higher rank. Which banner are you living under today—are you actively struggling for the truth in your life, or are you merely a passive believer? What is one specific struggle you can take up this week to prove your allegiance?

Definition of Qarn and Tabi'i
The commentator defines 'qarn' and states the conditions for being a tabi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical meaning of 'qarn' and the technical definition of a 'tabi'i', including the conditions required for each.

Core Idea
A generation connects people through shared time; a Follower is defined by a simple meeting with a Companion.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed clarifies two key terms: 'qarn' (generation/century) and 'tabi'i' (Follower). Understanding these terms is essential for grasping the chain of transmission in Islamic knowledge. The commentator explains both the linguistic and technical meanings, and resolves a dispute about the conditions for being a tabi'i.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: first the definition of qarn, then the definition of tabi'i.
The passage uses technical terms like 'tamyiz' which are explained in the commentary.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where the commentator defines 'qarn' (two possible meanings) and where he defines 'tabi'i'. Note the dispute about whether tamyiz is required for a tabi'i.
3
Understand the relied-upon view
The commentator states that the relied-upon view is that a customary meeting suffices for being a tabi'i, and that tamyiz is not required. Compare this with the condition for a Companion.
This connects to the broader study of hadith sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقُرون جمع قَرْن ومعناه أهل زمانٍ واحدٍ متقارب اشتركوا في أمرٍ من الأمور المقصودة، كالصحابة فإنهم اشتركوا في الصحبة وهكذا مَنْ بعدهم، وقيل: معناه: الزمن الذي اشترك أهله في الأمر المذكور، وسُمِّيَ قَرْناً لأنه يَقْرِنُ أُمَّةٌ بأمَّةٍ وعالماً بعالم وعلى الأول فلا تقدير في كلام المصنف، وعلى الثاني ففي كلامه تقدير مضاف؛ أي: أهل القرون كما قدَّره الشارح في حل المتن . وقوله : (فَاسْتَمِعْ) تكملة. وقوله : (فَتابِعِي) بإسكان الياء مخففة يفيد أن رتبة التابعين تلي رتبة الصحابة من غير تراخ كبير، ولذلك عبر بالفاء المفيدة للترتيب والتعقيب. والتابعي من اجتمع بالصحابي اجتماعاً متعارفاً، ولا يُشترط فيه طول الاجتماع كما في الصحابي مع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وهذا ما صححه ابن الصلاح والنووي، وهو المعتمد. والطريقة المشهورة: أنه يُشترط التمييز في التابعي دون الصحابي، والمعتمد عندنا عدم اشتراطه في التابعي، كما لا يشترط في الصحابي.
And 'al-qurun' is the plural of 'qarn', and its meaning is: the people of a single close time period who shared in a matter of intended matters, like the Companions—they shared in companionship, and likewise those after them. And it is said: its meaning is: the time period whose people shared in the mentioned matter, and it was called 'qarn' because it connects a community with a community and a scholar with a scholar. According to the first, there is no ellipsis in the author's speech; according to the second, there is an omitted genitive, i.e., 'the people of the centuries' as the commentator assumed in resolving the text. And his saying: 'fa-stami‘' is a completion. And his saying: 'fa-tābi‘ī' with a silent yā', lightened, indicates that the rank of the Followers follows the rank of the Companions without much delay, and therefore he used the fā' indicating arrangement and immediate succession. And the Follower (tābi‘ī) is one who met a Companion in a customary meeting, and lengthy association is not required, as is the case for the Companion with the Prophet ﷺ. This is what Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ and al-Nawawī corrected, and it is the relied-upon view. The well-known method: that discernment (tamyīz) is required for the Follower but not for the Companion, and the relied-upon view with us is that it is not required for the Follower, just as it is not required for the Companion.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and technical definition. The commentator first explains the word 'qarn' (century/generation): it can refer either to the people of a specific time period who share a common characteristic (like companionship), or to the time period itself. The term 'qarn' is derived from 'qarana' (to connect) because it connects one generation to another. The commentator then notes that depending on the interpretation, there may or may not be an ellipsis in the author's phrase. Next, he explains the author's word 'fa-tābi‘ī' (so follow me), noting that the lightened ya' indicates the Followers (tābi‘ūn) come directly after the Companions. He then gives the definition of a tābi‘ī: one who meets a Companion in a customary meeting, without requiring long association (unlike the condition for a Companion's meeting with the Prophet). This is the view of Ibn al-Ṣalāḥ and al-Nawawī, and it is the relied-upon position. There is a dispute about whether 'tamyīz' (discernment/puberty) is required for a tābi‘ī; the commentator states the relied-upon view is that it is not required, just as it is not required for a Companion.

Key Terms
قرن
تابعي
صحابي
تمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a tabi'i must have extensive interaction with a Companion or must have reached puberty.
Correction: A single customary meeting suffices, and tamyiz (discernment) is not required.

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon condition for being a tābi‘ī according to this passage?
A) Meeting a Companion in a customary meeting
B) Having long association with a Companion
C) Having reached the age of discernment
D) Being known as a scholar

Key Takeaway
The term 'qarn' refers to people of a close time period sharing a characteristic, and a tabi'i is one who meets a Companion in a customary meeting, without requiring lengthy association or puberty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The term 'qarn' connects communities across time. What are you doing to bridge the gap between your generation and the next, ensuring the transmission of faith and knowledge?

Does the fact that the relied-upon view does not require puberty for being a tābi‘ī challenge your assumptions about who can learn from and be connected to the righteous? Are you excluding someone from spiritual companionship due to age or perceived lack of maturity?

The Best of the Followers and Ranking of Later Generations
The commentator identifies the best Follower and discusses the ranking of successive generations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the identity of the best Follower and the scholarly discussion about the relative merits of later generations based on prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
The best generation after the Companions is the Followers, with Uways al-Qarani as their best.

This passage from the Tuhfat al-Murid commentary addresses two subtle issues: who is the most virtuous among the Followers, and how later generations compare in rank. The commentator anchors the discussion in prophetic hadith while noting divergent scholarly views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the best Follower
Read the first part where the commentator identifies Uways al-Qarani as the best male Follower and Ḥafṣa bint Sīrīn as the best female Follower, noting a difference of opinion.
Uways al-Qarani is a well-known figure among early Muslims.
2
Understand the grammatical clarification
Notice how the commentator explains the author's phrase 'fa-tābi‘un li-man tabi‘' and why an explicit mention was used instead of a pronoun.
This shows the commentator's attention to linguistic precision.
3
Connect to the hadith on best generations
Read the hadith about the best three generations and understand its apparent meaning: that after the first three, all later generations are equal in virtue.
This is a famous hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Examine the two scholarly views
Compare the equality view with the degradation view, which holds that each generation is worse than the one before, supported by another hadith.
5
Reflect on the conflicting evidence
Consider the commentator's mention of a conflicting hadith about rain and his conclusion that observation (al-‘iyān) supports the degradation view.
This shows how scholars reconcile different textual evidences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفضلُ التابعين : أويس القرني (١) ، كما أن أفضل التابعيات: حفصة بنت سيرين، على خلافٍ في المسألة. وقوله : (فَتَابَعُ لِمَنْ تَبِعْ)، يفيد أن رُتبة أتباع التابعين تلي رتبة التابعين من غير تراخٍ كبيرٍ، كما مرَّ في الذي قبله، وفي كلامه إظهار في مقام الإضمار؛ إذ كان مقتضى الظاهر أن يقول فتابع له، ويكون الضمير عائداً على التابعي، والأصل في الترتيب الذي أفاده كلام المصنف قوله : «خيرُ أمتي القَرْن الذي يلونني ثم الذين يلونهم ثم الذين يلونهم» (٢) وظاهره أن ما بعد القرون الثلاثة سواء في الفضيلة وذهب جماعة إلى تفاوت بقية القرون بالشبقية، فكل قَرْنٍ أفضل من الذي بعده إلى يوم القيامة، لحديث: «ما من يوم إلا والذي بعده شرٌّ منه، وإنما يُسرع بخياركم» (۳) ، لكن قد ورد: «مثل هذه الأمة مثلُ المطر لا يُدرى أوله خير أم آخره» (٤) ، والعيانُ والعيان قاض بذلك.
And the best of the Followers (tābi‘ūn) is Uways al-Qarani, just as the best of the female Followers is Ḥafṣa bint Sīrīn, with a difference of opinion on the matter. And his saying: 'fa-tābi‘un li-man tabi‘' indicates that the rank of the Followers of the Followers (atbā‘ al-tābi‘īn) follows the rank of the Followers without much delay, as was mentioned in the previous. And in his speech there is an explicit mention in place of a pronoun, for the apparent requirement would be to say 'fa-tābi‘un lahu' and the pronoun would refer back to the Follower. The basis for the ordering indicated by the author's speech is his saying: 'The best of my community is the generation that follows me, then those who follow them, then those who follow them.' Its apparent meaning is that what comes after the three generations is equal in virtue. And a group held that the remaining generations differ with precedence, so every generation is better than the one after it until the Day of Resurrection, due to the hadith: 'There is no day except that what comes after it is worse than it, and only your best ones will be taken quickly.' However, it has been narrated: 'The example of this community is like the rain: it is not known whether its beginning is better or its end.' Observation clearly supports that.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage discusses the ranking of the Followers (tābi‘ūn) and later generations. First, the commentator states that the best male Follower is Uways al-Qarani and the best female Follower is Ḥafṣa bint Sīrīn, though there is scholarly dispute. Then he explains the author's phrase 'fa-tābi‘un li-man tabi‘' (so the Follower is for the one who follows) which shows that the generation after the Followers (atbā‘ al-tābi‘īn) follows them in rank without a great gap, and he notes a grammatical subtlety: the author used an explicit mention instead of a pronoun for clarity. The main basis for this ordering is the hadith: 'The best of my community is the generation that follows me, then those who follow them, then those who follow them.' The apparent meaning is that after the first three generations, all later generations are equal in virtue. However, some scholars held that every later generation is worse than the one before until the Day of Resurrection, citing another hadith: 'There is no day except that which comes after it is worse.' But the commentator notes a conflicting hadith comparing the community to rain, and concludes that real-world observation (al-‘iyān) decides the matter, suggesting that later generations are indeed worse.

Key Terms
أفضل التابعين
أويس القرني
أتباع التابعين
القرون الثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about the best three generations implies that all later centuries are equal in virtue.
Correction: While the apparent meaning of that hadith suggests equality, other hadith and scholarly opinion hold that later generations are progressively worse. The commentator notes real-world observation as evidence for degradation.

Checkpoint
What is the apparent meaning of the hadith 'خير أمتي القرن الذي يلونني ثم الذين يلونهم ثم الذين يلونهم' regarding generations after the first three?
A) They are all equal in virtue
B) They are progressively worse
C) They are better than the first three
D) Only the first three are virtuous

Key Takeaway
The best of the Followers is Uways al-Qarani, and while the apparent meaning of the three-generations hadith suggests equality later, other evidence indicates later generations are progressively worse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If each generation after the three is progressively worse, how does that reality shape your commitment to following the Sunnah in an age of increasing distance from the prophetic example? What specific practices will you guard more carefully because of this?
?
Uways al-Qarani never met the Prophet, yet he became the best of the Followers. What does this tell you about your own potential to reach the highest ranks of piety without having seen the Prophet? What is holding you back from that level of devotion?
?
The hadith says 'only your best ones will be taken quickly' when times are bad. If you were taken today, would you be among the best or the complacent? What one change can you make starting today to ensure you are accounted among the righteous in a time of decline?

The Best Companions: The Caliphs in Order
The commentator identifies the best Companions as the caliphs and explains the caliphate's duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the best Companions are those who held the caliphate, and that the caliphate lasted thirty years before becoming a monarchy.

Core Idea
The best Companions are the rightly guided caliphs of the thirty-year caliphate.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid explains the poet's line about the best Companions being those who undertook the caliphate. It clarifies that these are the first four caliphs and provides a prophetic hadith distinguishing the caliphate from later monarchical rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a statement, a definition, a hadith, and an explanation.
The footnote markers (0) and (٦) are part of the original text.
2
Identify who is meant
The commentator says 'the best of them' refers to those who held the great caliphate. Identify why this specifically points to the first four caliphs.
The great caliphate is defined as deputyship from the Prophet.
3
Understand the hadith
The Prophet said the caliphate after him is thirty years, then it becomes a 'biting kingdom'. Understand what 'biting' means metaphorically.
The commentator explains it as oppressive and constricting.
4
Reflect on the ranking
This passage sets the order of the Companions: the best are the caliphs, and among them the order is based on their rule within the thirty-year period.
This implies the ranking of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَخَيْرُهُمْ مَنْ وُلِّيَ الخِلافَة :أي وأفضل الصحابة النفر الذي ولي الخلافة (0) العظمى، وهي النيابة عن النبي ﷺ في مصالح المسلمين، وقد قدر صلى الله عليه وسلم مدتها عموم بقوله : «الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون - أي: سنة - ثم تصير مُلكاً عَضُوضاً (٦) أي : ذا عَضُ وتضييق ؛ لأن الملوك يضرُّون بالرعية حتى كأنهم يعضون عضا، فالمراد أنه ذو تضييق ومشقة على الرعية.
His saying: 'And the best of them is the one who undertook the caliphate': meaning, the best of the Companions are the group who undertook the great caliphate, which is the deputyship from the Prophet ﷺ for the welfare of the Muslims. And the Prophet ﷺ estimated its duration in general in his saying: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years — that is, years — then it will become a biting kingdom (mulkan ‘aḍūḍan),' meaning: having biting and constriction, because kings harm the subjects until they are as if they are biting with teeth. So the meaning is that it is constricting and difficult for the subjects.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage explains the poem's line 'وَخَيْرُهُمْ مَنْ وُلِّيَ الخِلافَة' (And the best of them is the one who undertook the caliphate). The commentator clarifies that 'the best of them' refers to the Companions who held the great caliphate, which is the deputyship of the Prophet for managing the Muslims' affairs. He then quotes the hadith from al-Tirmidhi: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years, then it will become a biting kingdom.' The commentator explains 'biting' (ʿaḍūḍ) as a metaphor for the kings' oppressive rule, causing hardship and constriction for the people. Thus, the caliphate is a virtuous period, but after thirty years it transforms into monarchy, which is less virtuous. This passage sets up the ranking of the Companions: the best are the first four caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali), as they were the ones who held this caliphate within the thirty-year period.

Key Terms
خيرهم
الخلافة العظمى
ملك عضوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking 'the best of them' refers to all Companions equally.
Correction: The passage specifies it refers specifically to those who held the caliphate, i.e., the first four caliphs.

Checkpoint

A) Thirty years
B) Forty years
C) Until the Day of Judgment
D) Unknown

Key Takeaway
The best Companions are the four rightly guided caliphs whose reign was the prophetic caliphate of thirty years.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The hadith teaches that after thirty years, rule becomes oppressive. In your own life, do you seek to be a source of ease for others, or do you sometimes act in ways that 'bite' those under your care? How can you change?
?
The caliphate was for the welfare of the Muslims. Whose welfare are you prioritizing today—your own or others? Will you commit to a specific act this week that puts someone else's well-being above your own comfort?

Definition of Wali and Karamah
The author defines the saint (wali) and the nature of saintly wonder (karamah)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of wali (saint) and karamah (saintly wonder) and the conditions that distinguish them from miracles and prophethood.

Core Idea
A saint is not sinless but repentant; a wonder is not a miracle but an extraordinary event.

In this passage, Sanusi provides precise definitions for two interrelated but distinct terms: wali and karamah. Understanding these definitions is essential for avoiding common misconceptions about the status of saints in Islamic theology. The author carefully distinguishes the saint's condition from that of prophets and clarifies the nature of extraordinary events that occur through them.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of wali and karamah
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author defines wali first, giving multiple conditions, and then defines karamah.
Pay attention to the two meanings of the word 'wali'.
2
Analyze the conditions for sainthood
The passage lists several attributes of a wali: knowledge of Allah, constant obedience, avoiding sins with repentance, and not showing false piety. Also, the name 'wali' has two meanings: Allah takes care of him, and he worships Allah continuously.
Notice that a wali is not infallible; he repents.
3
Understand the definition of karamah
Karamah is an extraordinary event that occurs through a righteous, orthodox believer. It is distinct from a prophet's miracle (mu'jiza).
The author refers back to earlier discussion on extraordinary matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأولياء جمع وَلِيّ، وهو العارف بالله تعالى وبصفاته حسب الإمكان، المواظب على الطاعات المجتنب للمعاصي بمعنى أنه لا يرتكب معصية بدون توبة، وليس المراد أنه لا تقع منه معصية بالكلية؛ إذ ليس معصوماً . وقولهم : (لا) يكذب (الولي أي بلسان حاله بأن يُظهر خلاف ما يُبطن المعرضُ عن الانهماك في اللذات والشهوات المباحة، وأما أصل التناول فلا مانع منه، لا سيما إذا كان بقصد التقوي على العبادة، وسُمي (وليا) ؛ لأن الله تولَّى أمره فلم يكله إلى نفسه ولا إلى غيره لحظةٌ، ولأنه يتولَّى عبادة الله على الدوام من غير أن يتخللها عصيان، وكلا المعنيين واجب تحققه حتى يكون الوليُّ عندنا وليا في نفس الأمر. والكرامةُ : أمرٌ خارقٌ للعادة يظهر على يد عبد ظاهر الصلاح ملتزم لمتابعة نبي بشريعته، مصحوب بصحيح الاعتقاد والعمل الصالح، علم بها أو لم يُعلم بها ، وسبق ما يتعلق بخوارق العادة عند المعجزات.
And al-awliya' (plural of wali) is the gnostic of Allah Almighty and His attributes to the extent possible, the one constant in acts of obedience, avoiding sins in the sense that he does not commit a sin without repentance, and it is not intended that no sin occurs from him at all, for he is not infallible. And their saying: '(no) lie (of the wali)' means by his state that he shows contrary to what he conceals, turning away from immersion in permissible pleasures and desires. As for the original indulgence, there is no prohibition, especially if the intention is to strengthen oneself for worship. And he is called 'wali' because Allah has taken charge of his affair, not entrusting him to himself or to others for a moment, and because he continuously undertakes the worship of Allah without any disobedience intermixing. Both meanings must be realized for him to be a wali in reality according to us. And al-karamah is an extraordinary matter that appears at the hand of a servant who is outwardly righteous, committed to following a prophet in his law, accompanied by correct belief and righteous action, whether he knows of it or not. And what relates to extraordinary matters has preceded in the discussion of miracles.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms: wali (saint) and karamah (wonder/saintly miracle). The definition of wali is given in several parts: (1) a wali is a gnostic of Allah and His attributes to the best of human ability; (2) he is constant in acts of obedience and avoids sins, but not sinlessly—he repents when he errs; (3) the statement that 'no lie of the wali' means his outer state does not contradict his inner state, and he refrains from excessive indulgence in permissible pleasures, though moderate use for strength in worship is permitted. The reason for the name 'wali' is twofold: Allah takes charge of his affairs (tawalla amrahu) so he is not left to himself, and he himself continuously undertakes (yatawalla) the worship of Allah without disobedience. Both meanings must be true for him to be a genuine wali. The definition of karamah follows: it is an extraordinary event (amr khariq li-l-ada) that occurs at the hand of a servant who is outwardly righteous, committed to following a prophet's law, with correct belief and righteous deeds, regardless of whether the servant himself is aware of it. The author notes that the general discussion of extraordinary matters (khawariq al-ada) has already been covered in the section on miracles (mu'jizat).

Key Terms
ولي
كرامة
ولاية
خارق للعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saints are sinless and never commit sins.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that a wali is not infallible; he repents when he sins. The statement 'not intended that no sin occurs from him at all' clarifies that saints are not sinless.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two meanings must be realized for a person to be a wali in reality?
A) He must be infallible and perform miracles.
B) Allah takes charge of his affairs, and he constantly worships Allah without disobedience.
C) He must avoid all permissible pleasures and be outwardly pious.
D) He must be a scholar and a companion of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The wali is a repentant servant under Allah's care, and the karamah is an extraordinary event that occurs through such a servant, distinct from prophetic miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The wali is not sinless but repents. Think of a sin you have committed repeatedly. Have you truly repented and changed, or do you assume you will sin again without intention to stop? What step can you take today to break that cycle?
?
The source says the wali is not immersed in permissible pleasures. Which halal indulgence (e.g., food, entertainment, social media) weakens your focus on worship? Will you set a limit for the next week to strengthen your connection with Allah?
?
The wali is one whom Allah does not leave to himself for a moment. In what area of your life are you acting as if you are in control—planning, worrying, or relying on your own strength? How can you hand that back to Allah today?

Raqib and Atid: The Two Recording Angels
Commentary on 'katibun' and the two recording angels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature and role of the two recording angels (Raqib and Atid) as described in the commentary, including their distinction from guardian angels and their continuous activity after death.

Core Idea
Each recording angel is both a guardian and a witness, and their work continues after death.

This lesson examines the commentary on the terms 'writers' and 'chosen' in the poem, clarifying the identity and function of the two angels assigned to every person. The author refutes a common misunderstanding about their roles and explains their continued activity at the grave.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the author's discussion about the angels.
2
Understand the grammatical connection
The author explains that 'writers' is conjuncted to 'guardians' and that 'chosen' is an adjective. This grammatical analysis helps establish that the two groups are distinct.
3
Focus on the role of each angel
Note that each of the two angels is both a guardian (raqib) and a witness (atid), contrary to a common misunderstanding that each has a separate role.
4
Reflect on the afterlife function
The angels remain with the person after death. For a believer, they glorify Allah and write reward; for a disbeliever, they curse. This underscores the gravity of one's faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وكَاتِبُونَ) عطفٌ على قوله : (حافظون). وقوله : ( خِيْرَةُ) بالرفع صفة لقوله : (كاتبون)؛ أي: مختارون؛ لأن الله تعالى اختارهم لذلك، وقد علمت أنه وقع خلاف في هذا العطف، فقيل: للتغاير، وقيل: للتفسير، والحق الأول، والمراد بالجمع ما فوق الواحد ؛ لأن كل واحد من العباد إنما عليه ملكان وكل منهما رقيب؛ أي حافظ وعتيد ؛ أي: حاضر ، لا كما قد يُتوهم من أن أحدهما رقيب والآخر عتيد، وهما لا يتغيران ما دام حيا، فإذا مات يقومان على قبره يسبحان ويهللان ويكبران ويكتبان ثوابه له إلى يوم القيامة إن كان مؤمناً ، ويلعنانه إلى يوم القيامة إن كان كافراً .
His statement: ‘And writers’ is conjuncted to his statement: ‘Guardians’. And his statement: ‘Chosen’ in the nominative case is an adjective to his statement: ‘writers’; i.e. selected, because Allah Most High selected them for that. And you know that there occurred a difference regarding this conjunction: it is said it indicates distinctness, and it is said it indicates explanation, and the correct is the first. And what is meant by the plural is more than one, because each of the servants has two angels, and each of them is raqib (guardian) and atid (present), not as is imagined by some that one of them is raqib and the other atid. They do not change as long as he is alive. When he dies, they stand at his grave glorifying, magnifying, and praising Allah, and they write his reward for him until the Day of Resurrection if he is a believer, and they curse him until the Day of Resurrection if he is a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage provides commentary on the words ‘writers’ (kātibūn) and ‘chosen’ (khīrah) from the poem. The author explains the grammatical relationship and the theological meaning. He states that ‘writers’ is a conjunction to ‘guardians’, indicating that these are two distinct groups of angels, not the same, contrary to one scholarly opinion that it is merely explanatory. The plural ‘writers’ means more than one, and each person has two specific angels. Each of these two angels is both raqib (a guardian) and atid (present), meaning they combine these functions. The author refutes a misunderstanding that one angel is exclusively raqib and the other exclusively atid. These two angels remain with the person throughout life and do not change. After death, they continue their role at the grave: for a believer, they glorify Allah and write the reward of the believer until the Day of Judgment; for a disbeliever, they curse him until that day. This underscores the perpetual accountability and the gravity of one's state at death.

Key Terms
كاتبون
رقيب
عتيد
خيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the two angels have distinct roles: one is a guardian (raqib) and the other is a witness (atid).
Correction: Each of the two angels is both a guardian and a witness; they do not have separate functions.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens to the two recording angels when a believer dies?
A) They leave him immediately.
B) They stand at his grave glorifying and writing his reward until Judgment Day.
C) They curse him until Judgment Day.
D) They return to the heavens.

Key Takeaway
The two recording angels, each of whom is both a guardian and a witness, stay with a person throughout life and continue their work at the grave, recording reward for believers and cursing for disbelievers until Judgment Day.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are never alone; each moment you are watched by two angels who are both guardians and witnesses. What secret thought or action today would you be ashamed for them to record? Will you repent and change it now?
?
If you knew that after your death your angels will either glorify Allah and record reward for you or curse you based on your faith, what would you do today to ensure they are on your side?
?
The angels continue their work even after you die. How does this truth affect your concern for what happens to you in the grave? What can you do right now to improve your standing there?

The Reality of the Soul: The Maliki View and Preferred Definition
Presenting the Maliki position and the preferred view of Imam al-Haramayn on the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debated nature of the soul according to the Maliki school and the preferred definition by Imam al-Haramayn.

Core Idea
The soul is a subtle body, not a physical form like the outward body.

The author begins by presenting the position of the Maliki school regarding the soul's reality. He then cites Imam al-Haramayn's view, which is considered the most correct on this issue. This lesson will help you grasp the key debate and the resolution based on transmitted texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the core ideas.
2
Identify the Maliki view
Find the description of the soul as a body with form, including the quoted statement from Abd al-Rahim ibn Khalid.
Notice the explicit mention of hands, feet, eyes, and head.
3
Identify the preferred view
Locate the quote from Imam al-Haramayn describing the soul as a subtle, transparent body intertwined with the physical body.
Compare this with the Maliki view and note why the author calls the Maliki view 'not the chosen one.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٩٤ - لِمالِكِ هِي صُوْرَةٌ كَالجَسَدِ فَحَسْبُكَ النَّصُّ بِهَذَا السَّنَدِ قوله : لَكِنْ وُجِدَا لِمالِكِ هِي صُوْرَةٌ كالجَسَدِ) بسكون الياء لغة في (هي) بفتحها ؛ أي : لكن وجد لأهل مذهب مالك ممَّن خاض في بيان حقيقة الروح هي : جسم ذو صورة كصورة الجسد في الشكل والهيئة، فإنَّ أصبغ نقل عن ابن القاسم عن عبد الرحيم بن خالد قال : (الروحُ ذو جسد ويدين ورجلين وعينين ورأس تُسَلُّ من الجسدِ سَلَّا)؛ وإنما نسبه المصنّف لمالك لاستنادهم إليه في ذلك، وما ذكر من الخوض في الروح هو غير المختار . قال النووي: وأصح ما قيل فيها على هذه الطريقة ما قاله إمام الحرمين : (إنها جسم لطيف شفافٌ مُشتبك بالجسم ؛ كاشتباك الماء بالعود الأخضر، فتكون البدن). سارية في جميع وقيل : مقرها البطنُ ، وقيل : القلب وقيل : بقرب القلب، والصواب ما قاله إمام الحرمين: (وهذا في حالة الحياة، وأما بعد الموت فأرواح السعداء بأفنية القبور على الصحيح). وقيل : عند آدم عليه الصلاة والسلام في سماء الدنيا لكن لا دائماً، فلا ينافي أنها تسرح حيث شاءت ؛ وأما أرواح الكفار [ف]في سِجِّين في الأرض السابعة السفلى محبوسة. وقيل : أرواح السعداء بالجابية في الشام. وقيل : ببئر زمزم وأرواح الكفار ببئر بَرَهُوت (۱) في حضرموت التي هي مدينة في اليمن . وقوله : (فَحَسْبُكَ النَّصُّ بِهَذَا السَّنَدِ أي: إذا علمت النقل عن أهل مذهب مالك بالخوض في حقيقتها، فيكفيك في الخوض النصُّ عنهم حال كونه ملتبساً بهذا القول المسند إليهم من مُلابسة العام للخاص، فلا تخض بأكثر منه، فالمراد بالسند المسند إلى أهل مذهب مالك، وإن كان في الأصل هو الطريق الموصلة للحديث، وتلك الطريق هي الرجال الذين يروون الحديث. فإن قيل : يرد على ذلك أنه إذا قطع عضو حيوان لزم قطع نظيره من الروح، أجيب بأن لطافتها تقتضي سرعة انجذابها وانضمامها من ذلك العضو المقطوع قبل انفصاله أو سرعة الالتحام بعد القطع، وهذا يقتضي انقطاع الروح ثم تلتحم سريعاً، والأول يقتضي عدم انقطاعها فهو أولى؛ لأن الأصل عدم الانقطاع. فإن قيل : كيف يخوضون في الروح مع أن الآية دالة على عدم الخوض فيها، حيث أمر الله النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بأن يقول : وقُلِ الرُّوحُ مِنْ أَمْرِ رَبِّي [الإسراء: ٨٥]، أجيب : بأنه إنما أُمر عليه الصلاة والسلام بترك الجواب تصديقاً لما في كتب اليهود من أن الإمساك عن ذلك علامات نبوته وأدلَّةِ رسالته .
94. For Malik, it is a form like the body; the transmitted text with this chain is sufficient for you. His saying: 'But it is found for Malik: it is a form like the body' – with sukun on the ya as a dialect of (hiya) with fatha. Meaning: It is found among the followers of Malik's school who delved into explaining the reality of the soul that it is a body having a form like the form of the body in shape and appearance. For Asbagh narrated from Ibn al-Qasim from Abd al-Rahim ibn Khalid who said: 'The soul has a body, hands, feet, eyes, and a head; it is pulled out of the body with a pulling motion.' The author attributes it to Malik because they rely on him in that. What is mentioned of delving into the soul is not the preferred view. Al-Nawawi said: The most correct thing said on this method is what Imam al-Haramayn said: 'It is a subtle, transparent body intertwined with the body like the intertwining of water with green wood, so it pervades the whole body.' And it is said: Its seat is the abdomen; and it is said: the heart; and it is said: near the heart. The correct view is what Imam al-Haramayn said: 'This is in the state of life. As for after death, the souls of the blessed are at the courtyards of graves, according to the sound view.' And it is said: [they are] with Adam, peace and blessings be upon him, in the lowest heaven, but not permanently, so it does not prevent that they roam wherever they wish. As for the souls of the disbelievers, they are confined in Sijjin in the lowest seventh earth. And it is said: The souls of the blessed are in al-Jabiya in Syria. And it is said: At the well of Zamzam, and the souls of the disbelievers are at the well of Barahut in Hadramawt, which is a city in Yemen. And his saying: 'So the transmitted text with this chain is sufficient for you' means: When you know the transmission from the followers of Malik's school about delving into its reality, the text from them suffices for you in delving, while it is clothed with this statement attributed to them, with the attachment of the general to the specific. So do not delve more than that. By 'chain' is meant the chain attributed to the followers of Malik's school, though originally it is the path connecting to the hadith, and that path is the men who narrate the hadith. If it is said: An objection arises against this: If a limb of an animal is cut, the cutting of its counterpart from the soul becomes necessary. The answer is: Its subtlety necessitates its rapid attraction and contraction from that severed limb before its separation, or rapid reattachment after severance. This would entail the soul being severed and then reattaching quickly, while the first [view] entails its non-severance, so it is more appropriate because the original principle is non-severance. If it is said: How can they delve into the soul while the verse indicates not delving into it, since Allah commanded the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, to say: 'And say: The soul is of the affair of my Lord' [Al-Isra: 85]? The answer is: He, peace and blessings be upon him, was only commanded to refrain from answering in confirmation of what is in the books of the Jews, that refraining from that is a sign of his prophethood and proof of his messengership.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part discussion on the reality of the soul (ruh). It begins by presenting the position of Malik's school that the soul is a body with a form similar to the physical body, supported by a narrated statement from Abd al-Rahim ibn Khalid via Asbagh. The commentator notes that this view is not the chosen one. Then, citing al-Nawawi, the preferred view of Imam al-Haramayn is given: the soul is a subtle, transparent body intertwined with the living body like water in green wood, pervading it. Various opinions on the location of souls after death are then listed: the souls of the blessed are at the courtyards of graves, or with Adam in the lowest heaven, or at al-Jabiya, or at Zamzam; the souls of disbelievers are confined in Sijjin or at the well of Barahut. The author explains that the phrase 'the transmitted text with this chain is sufficient for you' means that one may rely on the transmitted report from Malik's school regarding the soul's reality without delving further. Two objections are then raised and answered. The first objection: if a limb is cut, the soul's corresponding part should also be cut. The answer: the soul's subtlety causes it to rapidly contract from the limb before separation or rapidly reattach after cutting; it is more likely that it does not sever because the default assumption is non-severance. The second objection: the Quranic verse (17:85) forbids inquiry into the soul. The answer: the Prophet was commanded to refrain from answering to confirm the signs of his prophethood in Jewish scriptures, not because inquiry is inherently prohibited. Thus the discussion on the soul is permissible based on transmitted reports.

Key Terms
الروح (al-ruh: the soul)
جسم لطيف (jism latif: subtle body)
مالك (Malik: founder of the Maliki school)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, what is the nature of the soul?
A) A physical body like the human form
B) A subtle, transparent body intertwined with the physical body
C) A non-corporeal spirit without any physical attributes
D) A light residing in the heart only

Key Takeaway
The soul is a subtle body, not a physical form, and this understanding is based on transmitted reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul is described as a subtle body that intertwines with your physical body like water in green wood. In what areas of your life do you live as if your soul and body are separate, ignoring the soul's presence? What would change if you consciously acknowledged that your soul permeates every part of you?
?
The analogy of water in green wood means the soul and body are inseparable in this life. How does that challenge any tendency you have to prioritize your physical appearance or desires over the health of your soul? What is one concrete action you can take today to care for your soul as part of your body?
?
If the soul is pulled out of the body at death (as mentioned in the passage about the Maliki view), how does that affect your outlook on death? Are you prepared to release your soul, or are you clinging to your physical existence? What step can you take this week to strengthen your soul's connection to its Creator?

The Afterlife of Souls: Where Do They Reside?
Enumerating locations of souls after death and reinforcing reliance on transmitted reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various opinions on the location of souls after death and the author's concluding advice to rely on transmitted texts.

Core Idea
The souls of the blessed reside near their graves, while the souls of the disbelievers are confined.

After discussing the soul's nature, the author turns to its post-mortem state. He lists several opinions regarding where the souls of believers and disbelievers reside. Finally, he reiterates that one should not delve beyond what is transmitted from reliable sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation to get an overview of the multiple opinions.
2
Identify opinions for souls of the blessed
List the different places mentioned for the souls of believers: the courtyards of graves, with Adam, al-Jabiya, and the well of Zamzam.
Note which one is considered the sound view.
3
Identify opinions for souls of disbelievers
Find the two places: Sijjin in the lowest earth and the well of Barahut.
4
Understand the author's conclusion
Read the explanation of 'the transmitted text with this chain is sufficient for you' and understand why the author warns against delving beyond transmitted reports.
The author emphasizes relying on chain-backed reports.

Two Objections on the Soul and Their Answers
Answering two objections regarding the soul's corporeality and the permissibility of inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars answered objections against the corporeal nature of the soul and the apparent prohibition of discussing it.

Core Idea
The soul's subtlety and the specific context of the Quranic verse allow discussion of its nature.

This lesson addresses two objections raised against the view that the soul is a subtle body. The first concerns what happens when a limb is cut, and the second is based on the Quranic verse 'The soul is of the affair of my Lord.' The answers provided reconcile these objections with the transmitted position.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and its answer
Read the objection about limb cutting and the answer that relies on the soul's subtlety and rapid withdrawal or reattachment.
The default principle is non-severance.
2
Read the second objection and its answer
Read the objection citing Quran 17:85 and the answer that the Prophet's silence was a specific sign to the Jews.
3
Understand the reasoning
Reflect on how each answer uses transmitted texts and logical reasoning to defend the permissibility of discussing the soul's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And his saying: 'So the transmitted text with this chain is sufficient for you' means: When you know the transmission from the followers of Malik's school about delving into its reality, the text from them suffices for you in delving, while it is clothed with this statement attributed to them, with the attachment of the general to the specific. So do not delve more than that. By 'chain' is meant the chain attributed to the followers of Malik's school, though originally it is the path connecting to the hadith, and that path is the men who narrate the hadith. If it is said: An objection arises against this: If a limb of an animal is cut, the cutting of its counterpart from the soul becomes necessary. The answer is: Its subtlety necessitates its rapid attraction and contraction from that severed limb before its separation, or rapid reattachment after severance. This would entail the soul being severed and then reattaching quickly, while the first [view] entails its non-severance, so it is more appropriate because the original principle is non-severance. If it is said: How can they delve into the soul while the verse indicates not delving into it, since Allah commanded the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, to say: 'And say: The soul is of the affair of my Lord' [Al-Isra: 85]? The answer is: He, peace and blessings be upon him, was only commanded to refrain from answering in confirmation of what is in the books of the Jews, that refraining from that is a sign of his prophethood and proof of his messengership.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part discussion on the reality of the soul (ruh). It begins by presenting the position of Malik's school that the soul is a body with a form similar to the physical body, supported by a narrated statement from Abd al-Rahim ibn Khalid via Asbagh. The commentator notes that this view is not the chosen one. Then, citing al-Nawawi, the preferred view of Imam al-Haramayn is given: the soul is a subtle, transparent body intertwined with the living body like water in green wood, pervading it. Various opinions on the location of souls after death are then listed: the souls of the blessed are at the courtyards of graves, or with Adam in the lowest heaven, or at al-Jabiya, or at Zamzam; the souls of disbelievers are confined in Sijjin or at the well of Barahut. The author explains that the phrase 'the transmitted text with this chain is sufficient for you' means that one may rely on the transmitted report from Malik's school regarding the soul's reality without delving further. Two objections are then raised and answered. The first objection: if a limb is cut, the soul's corresponding part should also be cut. The answer: the soul's subtlety causes it to rapidly contract from the limb before separation or rapidly reattach after cutting; it is more likely that it does not sever because the default assumption is non-severance. The second objection: the Quranic verse (17:85) forbids inquiry into the soul. The answer: the Prophet was commanded to refrain from answering to confirm the signs of his prophethood in Jewish scriptures, not because inquiry is inherently prohibited. Thus the discussion on the soul is permissible based on transmitted reports.

Key Terms
الروح (al-ruh: the soul)
النص (al-nass: the transmitted text)
سند (sanad: chain of transmission)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse 'The soul is of the affair of my Lord' forbids all discussion about the soul.
Correction: The verse was specifically meant to silence the Jews who demanded an explanation as a test; it is not a blanket prohibition on exploring the soul's nature through transmitted reports.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why was the Prophet commanded to say 'The soul is of the affair of my Lord'?
A) Because the soul is unknowable to anyone
B) To confirm the signs of his prophethood in Jewish scriptures
C) To discourage all theological inquiry
D) Because he did not know the answer

Key Takeaway
Objections to discussing the soul are answered by appealing to its subtlety and the specific context of the Quranic verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When faced with an objection to your beliefs, do you immediately shut down discussion or do you seek the wisdom in the answer? This lesson shows that even the Prophet's silence was strategic. What objection are you currently avoiding, and how will you face it with the tools of inquiry?
?
The soul's subtlety is beyond physical cutting. In what ways are you neglecting your soul's subtle needs because you treat it as merely physical? What is one spiritual practice you will commit to this week to attend to that subtlety?
?
This lesson exposes that some 'forbidden' topics are actually permitted when understood correctly. Is there a topic you have avoided studying because you assumed it was off-limits? How can you investigate it properly now?

The Intellect: Definition and Types
The author defines the intellect and enumerates its first two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the intellect, its linguistic meaning, and the first two types: instinctive and acquired.

Core Idea
The intellect prevents its owner from straying like a camel's hobble.

The author begins by comparing the intellect to the soul and notes a disagreement about whether to speculate on its reality. He then explains its linguistic meaning as 'prevention' and classifies it into five types. This lesson covers the first two types: the instinctive intellect, which is an innate disposition, and the acquired intellect, gained through association with rational people.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's comparison of the intellect to the soul and his mention of disagreement.
The passage cuts off mid-classification; only the first two types are present.
2
Understand the disagreement about the intellect's reality
Identify the two views: one advocates delving into its reality, the other refraining. The author prefers refraining, as the intellect is an unseen matter.
3
Learn the linguistic meaning
The word 'aql comes from 'aqala al-ba'ir, meaning to hobble a camel, because the intellect prevents its owner from deviating from the straight path.
4
Study the first two types
The first type is instinctive (gharizi): an innate disposition preparing one for theoretical sciences. The second is acquired (kasbi): gained through associating with rational people.
The remaining three types are not present in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٩٥ - والعَقْلُ كَالرُّوحِ وَلَكِنْ قَرَّرُوا فِيْهِ خِلَافاً فَانْظُرَتْ ما فَشَرُوا قوله : (والعَقْلُ كَالرُّوحِ مبتدأ وخبر؛ أي: والعقل مثل الروح من حيث الخوض في بيان الحقيقة والوقف عن ذلك، واختلف كلام المصنف في الترجيح : فرجح في هداية المريد» طريق الخوض ، ورجّح في «الكبير» طريق الوقف، وهو المختار؛ لأنه المغيبات، وكلُّ ما هو كذلك فالأولى من كذلك فالأولى الكف عن الخوض فيه . لغة: وهو المنع ، من (عَقَلَ البعير : إذا منعه بالعِقال، وسُمِّي بذلك لمنعه صاحبه من العدول عن سواء السبيل. واعلم أن العقل على خمسة أنواع : الأول: غريزي ؛ وهو غريزة (۱) يُتهيَّأ بها (۲) لدرك العلوم النظرية، كما قاله الإسلام. والثاني : كسبي ؛ وهو ما يكتسبه الإنسان من معاشرة العقلاء.
95. And the intellect is like the soul, but they have established disagreement concerning it, so look at what they have explained. His saying: 'And the intellect is like the soul' is a subject and predicate; meaning: The intellect is like the soul in terms of delving into explaining its reality and refraining from that. The author's statements differ regarding which view is preferred: in 'Hidayat al-Murid' he preferred the way of delving, and in 'al-Kabir' he preferred the way of refraining, which is the chosen view, because it is among the unseen matters, and everything that is such, it is more appropriate to refrain from delving into it. Linguistically, it is 'al-man' (prevention), from 'aqala al-ba'ir' (to hobble the camel): when one prevents it with a hobble. It is named thus because it prevents its possessor from deviating from the straight path. Know that the intellect is of five types: First: Instinctive (gharizi); it is an instinct by which one is prepared to grasp theoretical sciences, as Islam has said. Second: Acquired (kasbi); it is what a person acquires from associating with rational people.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage defines the intellect (al-'aql) and discusses its nature. It begins by comparing the intellect to the soul: there is a disagreement about whether to delve into its reality or to refrain. The author notes that he himself gave conflicting rulings; in 'Hidayat al-Murid' he favored delving, but in 'al-Kabir' he favored refraining, which he considers the chosen view because the intellect is an unseen matter, and it is better to avoid speculation. Then he gives the linguistic definition of 'aql: it means prevention, derived from hobbling a camel, because it prevents its owner from straying. Finally, he begins a classification of the intellect into five types, but the text as present mentions only the first two: the instinctive intellect (al-'aql al-gharizi), which is an innate disposition that prepares one to grasp theoretical sciences, and the acquired intellect (al-'aql al-kasbi), which is gained through associating with rational people. The passage cuts off before the remaining three types are listed.

Key Terms
العقل (al-'aql: the intellect)
غريزي (gharizi: instinctive)
كسبي (kasbi: acquired)
المنع (al-man': prevention)
عقال (i'qal: hobble)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might assume that the intellect is purely rational and not subject to debate, and that its reality is well-known.
Correction: The author shows that the intellect's reality is disputed, and it is safer to refrain from speculation because it is an unseen matter.

Checkpoint

The intellect is both an innate disposition (instinctive) and a skill acquired through association with rational people.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Intellect: Its Nature and Linguistic Origin
The author discusses the intellect's hidden nature and its linguistic derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of 'aql and why scholars caution against delving into its true nature.

Core Idea
The intellect is a hidden reality; its name comes from the idea of preventing deviation.

The author begins by comparing the intellect to the soul, noting that both are mysterious realities about which scholars differ on whether to investigate. He then provides the linguistic root of 'aql, which means 'to prevent' or 'to hobble,' indicating that the intellect restrains its possessor from error. This lesson explores these foundational insights.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the intellect's similarity to the soul
Pay attention to the author's comparison and his two differing opinions on investigating the intellect's reality.
2
Understand the linguistic definition
Note the root word and its implication: the intellect prevents one from straying.
3
Reflect on the caution
Why is it better to refrain from investigating hidden matters like the intellect? Consider the concept of al-mughayyabat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والعَقْلُ كَالرُّوحِ مبتدأ وخبر؛ أي: والعقل مثل الروح من حيث الخوض في بيان الحقيقة والوقف عن ذلك، واختلف كلام المصنف في الترجيح : فرجح في «هداية المريد» طريق الخوض، ورجّح في «الكبير» طريق الوقف، وهو المختار؛ لأنه من المغيبات، وكلُّ ما هو كذلك فالأولى الكف عن الخوض فيه.
لغة: وهو المنع، من (عَقَلَ البعير) : إذا منعه بالعِقال، وسُمِّي بذلك لمنعه صاحبه من العدول عن سواء السبيل.
واعلم أن العقل على خمسة أنواع :
الأول: غريزي؛ وهو غريزة يُتَهيَّأ بها لدرك العلوم النظرية، كما قاله الإسلام.
والثاني : كسبي؛ وهو ما يكتسبه الإنسان من معاشرة العقلاء.
والثالث : عطائي؛ وهو ما يعطيه الله للمؤمن؛ ليهتدوا به إلى الإيمان.
والرابع: عقل الزهاد؛ وهو الذي يكون به الزهد.
والخامس شرفي؛ وهو عقل نبينا ﷺ؛ لأنه أشرف العقول.
His statement: (And the intellect is like the soul) is a subject and predicate; that is, the intellect is like the soul in terms of delving into the clarification of reality and refraining from that. The author's statement differed regarding the preponderance: he gave preponderance in 'Hidayat al-Murid' to the way of delving, and gave preponderance in 'al-Kabir' to the way of refraining, and that is the chosen view because it is among the hidden matters, and everything that is like that, the better course is to refrain from delving into it.
Linguistically: it is prevention, from 'he hobbled the camel' when he prevented it with the hobble; it is so named because it prevents its possessor from deviating from the straight path.
And know that the intellect is of five types:
First: instinctive (gharizi); it is an instinct by which one is prepared to grasp theoretical sciences, as Islam stated.
Second: acquired (kasbi); it is what a person acquires from associating with rational people.
Third: granted (ata'i); it is what Allah gives to the believer so that they may be guided thereby to faith.
Fourth: the intellect of the ascetics; it is that by which asceticism is achieved.
Fifth: honorary (sharafi); it is the intellect of our Prophet ﷺ, because it is the most honorable of intellects.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It begins by explaining the line 'And the intellect is like the soul'—meaning they are similar regarding whether to investigate their reality or refrain. The commentator notes that the author himself gave two different preponderances: in his earlier work 'Hidayat al-Murid' he favored investigation, but in 'al-Kabir' he favored refraining, and the latter is chosen because the intellect (like the soul) pertains to hidden matters (al-mughayyabat), so it is better to avoid delving into it. Then a linguistic definition is given: 'aql derives from 'aqala al-ba'ir' (to hobble a camel), and is so called because it prevents its possessor from deviating from the straight path. Finally, five types of intellect are enumerated: instinctive (gharizi) enabling theoretical knowledge, acquired (kasbi) through association, granted (ata'i) by Allah to believers, the intellect of ascetics (zuhhad) enabling asceticism, and the honorary (sharafi) intellect of the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ, being the most honorable. Each type is briefly explained.

Key Terms
العقل (al-'aql)
عقال (al-'iqāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the intellect likened to the soul?
A) Both are physical entities
B) Both are hidden realities
C) Both are acquired
D) Both are instinctive

Key Takeaway
The intellect is a hidden reality that restrains its possessor from error, as indicated by its linguistic root.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that the intellect's true nature is hidden and that we should refrain from delving into it. In what area of your faith have you been relying on your own reasoning beyond what has been revealed? Will you commit tonight to stop investigating that matter and instead focus on acting on what is clear?
?
The intellect is meant to prevent you from deviating from the straight path. Think of a recent situation where your desires or habits led you to a wrong choice. How did your intellect fail to hobble those desires? What specific step will you take tomorrow to let your intellect restrain you?
?
The author changed his view from investigating the intellect to refraining. Is there a theological or personal question you keep trying to answer fully but should instead accept as unknown? Are you willing to let go of that intellectual pursuit and trust God's wisdom? What would that look like in practice today?

The Five Types of Intellect in Islamic Thought
The author enumerates five types of intellect with brief definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the five types of intellect as enumerated by the author.

Core Idea
Intellect is not a single faculty; it appears as instinct, acquisition, divine gift, asceticism, and prophetic honor.

After defining the intellect broadly, the author enumerates five distinct types, each serving a different role: instinctive, acquired, granted by Allah, the intellect of the ascetics, and the ultimate prophetic intellect. This lesson breaks down each type and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of the five types

Understand the instinctive and acquired types

3
Examine the divinely granted and ascetic types
Allah grants a special intellect to believers, and the intellect of the ascetics enables their renunciation.
4
Appreciate the prophetic intellect
The intellect of Prophet Muhammad is the most honorable, setting the highest standard.

Disagreement on the Reality and Definitions of Intellect
The author explains the disagreement on the intellect and gives various scholarly definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that scholars disagreed on the exact definition of the intellect, but all Ahl al-Sunnah agree it is an accident, and you will learn three specific definitions.

Core Idea
The intellect is an accident, but its precise definition varies among scholars.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a point of dispute: the exact nature of the human intellect. While scholars differ in their definitions, they all agree that it is an accident. This lesson unpacks that disagreement and the key definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Disagreement
Read the opening lines about the corrective 'but' and the fact that scholars established disagreement concerning the intellect. Notice how the commentator clarifies the function of this phrase.
The passage suggests that disagreement about the intellect was widespread.
2
Identify the Consensus
Look for the statement that Ahl al-Sunnah are in agreement that the intellect is an accident ('arad). This is the foundational point.
Even though they differ on the definition, they all agree it is an accident, not a substance.
3
Learn the Three Definitions
Examine each definition: (1) knowledge of necessary sciences, (2) an instinct that leads to necessary knowledge, (3) an attribute that distinguishes good from evil. Note the scholars associated with each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولَكِنْ قَرَّرُوا فِيْهِ خِلَافاً أي لكن قرر العلماء في العقل خلافاً، ولا محل لهذا الاستدراك؛ لأنهم قرروا في الروح خلافاً أيضاً، فلعل (لكن) لمجرد التأكيد، ثم رأيت المصنف في شرحه قال: (ولكن ... إلى آخره) استدراك على طريقة الخائضين، فأشار إلى أنهم لم يتفقوا على حقيقةٍ معينةٍ، بل اختلفوا في بيانها. انتهى. فالاستدراك يُشعر بانتشار الخلاف وكثرته.
وقوله : (فَانْظُرَتْ ما فَسَّرُوا ) أي : فانظرن التفاسير التي ذكرها القوم في كتبهم، هذه المقدمة؛ لصغر حجمها. وأقوال أهل السنة متطابقة على عرضيته، فبعضهم قال: إنه من قبيل العلوم، وعرفه بأنه العلم ببعض العلوم الضرورية، كالعلم بوجوب تحيز الجرم، واستحالة عروه عن الحركة والسكون وجواز إحراق النار وغير ذلك، وهذا القول لإمام الحرمين وجماعة وبعضهم قال: (إنه ليس من قبيل العلوم، وعرفه بأنه غريزة؛ أي: طبيعة مغروزة يتبعها العلم بالضروريات عند سلامة الآلات)
وعرّفه الشيرازي بأنه صفةٌ يُميَّز بها بين الحسن والقبيح.
His statement: 'But they established disagreement concerning it'—meaning the scholars established disagreement concerning the intellect. There is no place for this correction because they also established disagreement concerning the soul. So perhaps 'but' is merely for emphasis. Then I saw that the author in his commentary said: ('But... etc.') is a correction to the method of those who delve (into hidden matters), indicating that they did not agree on a specific reality but differed in its explanation. End. So the correction suggests the spread and abundance of disagreement.
And his statement: 'So look at what they interpreted'—meaning look at the interpretations that the people mentioned in their books, this introduction because of its small size. And the statements of Ahl al-Sunnah are in agreement that it is an accident ('arad). Some said it belongs to the category of sciences, defining it as knowledge of some necessary sciences, such as the knowledge of the necessity of a body occupying space, the impossibility of it being devoid of motion and rest, the permissibility of fire burning, and other things. This is the statement of Imam al-Haramayn and a group. And some said: (It is not of the category of sciences, and defined it as an instinct; i.e., an ingrained nature that is followed by knowledge of necessities when the faculties are sound.)
And al-Shirazi defined it as an attribute by which one distinguishes between good and evil.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of two parts of the matn: 'but they established disagreement concerning it' and 'so look at what they interpreted'. First, the commentator notes that the word 'but' (lākin) appears as a correction to the method of those who delve into hidden matters, indicating that scholars did not agree on a single definition of the intellect but rather differed. He notes that there was also disagreement about the soul, so the 'but' may be for emphasis. Then, under 'so look at what they interpreted', the commentator urges the reader to examine the various interpretations given by scholars. He then presents the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah that the intellect is an accident ('arad). He gives three specific definitions: 1) Some (like Imam al-Haramayn) define it as knowledge of necessary sciences (e.g., that bodies occupy space, cannot be devoid of motion/rest, fire burns). 2) Others define it as an instinct (gharizah) that leads to necessary knowledge when the senses are sound. 3) Al-Shirazi defines it as a faculty that distinguishes good from evil. The passage thus highlights the diversity of opinion on the exact nature of the intellect while affirming its accidental character.

Key Terms
العقل (al-'aql)
العرض (al-'arad)
العلوم الضرورية (al-'ulum al-daruriyyah)
غريزة (gharizah)
صفة (sifah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the intellect is a substance (jawhar).
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah consensus is that it is an accident.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah regarding the intellect?
A) It is a substance.
B) It is an accident.
C) It is a body.
D) It is a divine attribute.

Key Takeaway
The intellect is an accident according to Ahl al-Sunnah, but scholars offer varying definitions: as knowledge of necessary sciences, as an instinct, or as a distinguishing attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that even scholars debated the exact definition of the intellect. How often do you simply assume your intellect is correct without examining its foundation? Today, identify one belief you hold that you consider 'obvious' and ask yourself: is it based on necessary knowledge or just habit? What will you do to verify it?
?
The intellect is said to distinguish good from evil. In the next hour, before you make a decision—however small—pause and consciously use your intellect to judge whether the action is good or evil. Are you willing to do that, or do you prefer to follow impulse? Why?
